java.util.Properties;\n48: import java.util.Set;\n49: import java.util.regex.Pattern;\n50: \n51: import static org.apache.kafka.common.utils.Utils.propsToMap;\n52: \n53: /**\n54:  * A client that consumes records from a Kafka cluster.\n55:  * <p>\n56:  * This client transparently handles the failure of Kafka brokers, and transparently adapts as topic partitions\n57:  * it fetches migrate within the cluster. This client also interacts with the broker to allow groups of\n58:  * consumers to load balance consumption using <a href=\"#consumergroups\">consumer groups</a>.\n59:  * <p>\n60:  * The consumer maintains TCP connections to the necessary brokers to fetch data.\n61:  * Failure to close the consumer after use will leak these connections.\n62:  * The consumer is not thread-safe. See <a href=\"#multithreaded\">Multi-threaded Processing</a> for more details.\n63:  *\n64:  * <h3>Cross-Version Compatibility</h3>\n65:  * This client can communicate with brokers that are version 0.10.0 or newer. Older or newer brokers may not support\n66:  * certain features. For example, 0.10.0 brokers do not support offsetsForTimes, because this feature was added\n67:  * in version 0.10.1. You will receive an {@link org.apache.kafka.common.errors.UnsupportedVersionException}\n68:  * when invoking an API that is not available on the running broker version.\n69:  * <p>\n70:  *\n71:  * <h3>Offsets and Consumer Position</h3>\n72:  * Kafka maintains a numerical offset for each record in a partition. This offset acts as a unique identifier of\n73:  * a record within that partition, and also denotes the position of the consumer in the partition. For example, a consumer\n74:  * which is at position 5 has consumed records with offsets 0 through 4 and will next receive the record with offset 5.\n75:  * Note that offsets are not guaranteed to be consecutive (such as compacted topic or when records have been produced\n76:  * using transactions). For example, if the consumer did read a record with offset 4, but 5 is not an offset\n77:  * with a record, its position might advance to 6 (or higher) directly. Similarly, if the consumer's position is 5,\n78:  * but there is no record with offset 5, the consumer will return the record with the next higher offset.\n79:  * There are actually two notions of position relevant to the user of the consumer:\n80:  * <p>\n81:  * The {@link #position(TopicPartition) position} of the consumer gives the offset of the next record that will be given\n82:  * out. It will be one larger than the highest offset the consumer has seen in that partition. It automatically advances\n83:  * every time the consumer receives messages in a call to {@link #poll(Duration)}.\n84:  * <p>\n85:  * The {@link #commitSync() committed position} is the last offset that has been stored securely. Should the\n86:  * process fail and restart, this is the offset that the consumer will recover to. The consumer can either automatically commit\n87:  * offsets periodically; or it can choose to control this committed position manually by calling one of the commit APIs\n88:  * (e.g. {@link #commitSync() commitSync} and {@link #commitAsync(OffsetCommitCallback) commitAsync}).\n89:  * <p>\n90:  * This distinction gives the consumer control over when a record is considered consumed. It is discussed in further\n91:  * detail below.\n92:  *\n93:  * <h3><a name=\"consumergroups\">Consumer Groups and Topic Subscriptions</a></h3>\n94:  *\n95:  * Kafka uses the concept of <i>consumer groups</i> to allow a pool of processes to divide the work of consuming and\n96:  * processing records. These processes can either be running on the same machine or they can be\n97:  * distributed over many machines to provide scalability and fault tolerance for processing. All consumer instances\n98:  * sharing the same {@code group.id} will be part of the same consumer group.\n99:  * <p>\n100:  * Each consumer in a group can dynamically set the list of topics it wants to subscribe to through one of the\n101:  * {@link #subscribe(Collection, ConsumerRebalanceListener) subscribe} APIs. Kafka will deliver each message in the\n102:  * subscribed topics to one process in each consumer group. This is achieved by balancing the partitions between all\n103:  * members in the consumer group so that each partition is assigned to exactly one consumer in the group. So if there\n104:  * is a topic with four partitions, and a consumer group with two processes, each process would consume from two partitions.\n105:  * <p>\n106:  * Membership in a consumer group is maintained dynamically: if a process fails, the partitions assigned to it will\n107:  * be reassigned to other consumers in the same group. Similarly, if a new consumer joins the group, partitions will be moved\n108:  * from existing consumers to the new one. This is known as <i>rebalancing</i> the group and is discussed in more\n109:  * detail <a href=\"#failuredetection\">below</a>. Group rebalancing is also used when new partitions are added\n110:  * to one of the subscribed topics or when a new topic matching a {@link #subscribe(Pattern, ConsumerRebalanceListener) subscribed regex}\n111:  * is created. The group will automatically detect the new partitions through periodic metadata refreshes and\n112:  * assign them to members of the group.\n113:  * <p>\n114:  * Conceptually you can think of a consumer group as being a single logical subscriber that happens to be made up of\n115:  * multiple processes. As a multi-subscriber system, Kafka naturally supports having any number of consumer groups for a\n116:  * given topic without duplicating data (additional consumers are actually quite cheap).\n117:  * <p>\n118:  * This is a slight generalization of the functionality that is common in messaging systems. To get semantics similar to\n119:  * a queue in a traditional messaging system all processes would be part of a single consumer group and hence record\n120:  * delivery would be balanced over the group like with a queue. Unlike a traditional messaging system, though, you can\n121:  * have multiple such groups. To get semantics similar to pub-sub in a traditional messaging system each process would\n122:  * have its own consumer group, so each process would subscribe to all the records published to the topic.\n123:  * <p>\n124:  * In addition, when group reassignment happens automatically, consumers can be notified through a {@link ConsumerRebalanceListener},\n125:  * which allows them to finish necessary application-level logic such as state cleanup, manual offset\n126:  * commits, etc. See <a href=\"#rebalancecallback\">Storing Offsets Outside Kafka</a> for more details.\n127:  * <p>\n128:  * It is also possible for the consumer to <a href=\"#manualassignment\">manually assign</a> specific partitions\n129:  * (similar to the older \"simple\" consumer) using {@link #assign(Collection)}. In this case, dynamic partition\n130:  * assignment and consumer group coordination will be disabled.\n131:  *\n132:  * <h3><a name=\"failuredetection\">Detecting Consumer Failures</a></h3>\n133:  *\n134:  * After subscribing to a set of topics, the consumer will automatically join the group when {@link #poll(Duration)} is\n135:  * invoked. The poll API is designed to ensure consumer liveness. As long as you continue to call poll, the consumer\n136:  * will stay in the group and continue to receive messages from the partitions it was assigned. Underneath the covers,\n137:  * the consumer sends periodic heartbeats to the server. If the consumer crashes or is unable to send heartbeats for\n138:  * a duration of {@code session.timeout.ms}, then the consumer will be considered dead and its partitions will\n139:  * be reassigned.\n140:  * <p>\n141:  * It is also possible that the consumer could encounter a \"livelock\" situation where it is continuing\n142:  * to send heartbeats, but no progress is being made. To prevent the consumer from holding onto its partitions\n143:  * indefinitely in this case, we provide a liveness detection mechanism using the {@code max.poll.interval.ms}\n144:  * setting. Basically if you don't call poll at least as frequently as the configured max interval,\n145:  * then the client will proactively leave the group so that another consumer can take over its partitions. When this happens,\n146:  * you may see an offset commit failure (as indicated by a {@link CommitFailedException} thrown from a call to {@link #commitSync()}).\n147:  * This is a safety mechanism which guarantees that only active members of the group are able to commit offsets.\n148:  * So to stay in the group, you must continue to call poll.\n149:  * <p>\n150:  * The consumer provides two configuration settings to control the behavior of the poll loop:\n151:  * <ol>\n152:  *     <li><code>max.poll.interval.ms</code>: By increasing the interval between expected polls, you can give\n153:  *     the consumer more time to handle a batch of records returned from {@link #poll(Duration)}. The drawback\n154:  *     is that increasing this value may delay a group rebalance since the consumer will only join the rebalance\n155:  *     inside the call to poll. You can use this setting to bound the time to finish a rebalance, but\n156:  *     you risk slower progress if the consumer cannot actually call {@link #poll(Duration) poll} often enough.</li>\n157:  *     <li><code>max.poll.records</code>: Use this setting to limit the total records returned from a single\n158:  *     call to poll. This can make it easier to predict the maximum that must be handled within each poll\n159:  *     interval. By tuning this value, you may be able to reduce the poll interval, which will reduce the\n160:  *     impact of group rebalancing.</li>\n161:  * </ol>\n162:  * <p>\n163:  * For use cases where message processing time varies unpredictably, neither of these options may be sufficient.\n164:  * The recommended way to handle these cases is to move message processing to another thread, which allows\n165:  * the consumer to continue calling {@link #poll(Duration) poll} while the processor is still working.\n166:  * Some care must be taken to ensure that committed offsets do not get ahead of the actual position.\n167:  * Typically, you must disable automatic commits and manually commit processed offsets for records only after the\n168:  * thread has finished handling them (depending on the delivery semantics you need).\n169:  * Note also that you will need to {@link #pause(Collection) pause} the partition so that no new records are received\n170:  * from poll until after thread has finished handling those previously returned.\n171:  *\n172:  * <h3>Usage Examples</h3>\n173:  * The consumer APIs offer flexibility to cover a variety of consumption use cases. Here are some examples to\n174:  * demonstrate how to use them.\n175:  *\n176:  * <h4>Automatic Offset Committing</h4>\n177:  * This example demonstrates a simple usage of Kafka's consumer api that relies on automatic offset committing.\n178:  * <p>\n179:  * <pre>\n180:  * {@code\n181:  *     Properties props = new Properties();\n182:  *     props.setProperty(\"bootstrap.servers\", \"localhost:9092\");\n183:  *     props.setProperty(\"group.id\", \"test\");\n184:  *     props.setProperty(\"enable.auto.commit\", \"true\");\n185:  *     props.setProperty(\"auto.commit.interval.ms\", \"1000\");\n186:  *     props.setProperty(\"key.deserializer\", \"org.apache.kafka.common.serialization.StringDeserializer\");\n187:  *     props.setProperty(\"value.deserializer\", \"org.apache.kafka.common.serialization.StringDeserializer\");\n188:  *     KafkaConsumer<String, String> consumer = new KafkaConsumer<>(props);\n189:  *     consumer.subscribe(Arrays.asList(\"foo\", \"bar\"));\n190:  *     while (true) {\n191:  *         ConsumerRecords<String, String> records = consumer.poll(Duration.ofMillis(100));\n192:  *         for (ConsumerRecord<String, String> record : records)\n193:  *             System.out.printf(\"offset = %d, key = %s, value = %s%n\", record.offset(), record.key(), record.value());\n194:  *     }\n195:  * }</pre>\n196:  *\n197:  * The connection to the cluster is bootstrapped by specifying a list of one or more brokers to contact using the\n198:  * configuration {@code bootstrap.servers}. This list is just used to discover the rest of the brokers in the\n199:  * cluster and need not be an exhaustive list of servers in the cluster (though you may want to specify more than one in\n200:  * case there are servers down when the client is connecting).\n201:  * <p>\n202:  * Setting {@code enable.auto.commit} means that offsets are committed automatically with a frequency controlled by\n203:  * the config {@code auto.commit.interval.ms}.\n204:  * <p>\n205:  * In this example the consumer is subscribing to the topics <i>foo</i> and <i>bar</i> as part of a group of consumers\n206:  * called <i>test</i> as configured with {@code group.id}.\n207:  * <p>\n208:  * The deserializer settings specify how to turn bytes into objects. For example, by specifying string deserializers, we\n209:  * are saying that our record's key and value will just be simple strings.\n210:  *\n211:  * <h4>Manual Offset Control</h4>\n212:  *\n213:  * Instead of relying on the consumer to periodically commit consumed offsets, users can also control when records\n214:  * should be considered as consumed and hence commit their offsets. This is useful when the consumption of the messages\n215:  * is coupled with some processing logic and hence a message should not be considered as consumed until it is completed processing.\n216: \n217:  * <p>\n218:  * <pre>\n219:  * {@code\n220:  *     Properties props = new Properties();\n221:  *     props.setProperty(\"bootstrap.servers\", \"localhost:9092\");\n222:  *     props.setProperty(\"group.id\", \"test\");\n223:  *     props.setProperty(\"enable.auto.commit\", \"false\");\n224:  *     props.setProperty(\"key.deserializer\", \"org.apache.kafka.common.serialization.StringDeserializer\");\n225:  *     props.setProperty(\"value.deserializer\", \"org.apache.kafka.common.serialization.StringDeserializer\");\n226:  *     KafkaConsumer<String, String> consumer = new KafkaConsumer<>(props);\n227:  *     consumer.subscribe(Arrays.asList(\"foo\", \"bar\"));\n228:  *     final int minBatchSize = 200;\n229:  *     List<ConsumerRecord<String, String>> buffer = new ArrayList<>();\n230:  *     while (true) {\n231:  *         ConsumerRecords<String, String> records = consumer.poll(Duration.ofMillis(100));\n232:  *         for (ConsumerRecord<String, String> record : records) {\n233:  *             buffer.add(record);\n234:  *         }\n235:  *         if (buffer.size() >= minBatchSize) {\n236:  *             insertIntoDb(buffer);\n237:  *             consumer.commitSync();\n238:  *             buffer.clear();\n239:  *         }\n240:  *     }\n241:  * }</pre>\n242:  *\n243:  * In this example we will consume a batch of records and batch them up in memory. When we have enough records\n244:  * batched, we will insert them into a database. If we allowed offsets to auto commit as in the previous example, records\n245:  * would be considered consumed after they were returned to the user in {@link #poll(Duration) poll}. It would then be\n246:  * possible\n247:  * for our process to fail after batching the records, but before they had been inserted into the database.\n248:  * <p>\n249:  * To avoid this, we will manually commit the offsets only after the corresponding records have been inserted into the\n250:  * database. This gives us exact control of when a record is considered consumed. This raises the opposite possibility:\n251:  * the process could fail in the interval after the insert into the database but before the commit (even though this\n252:  * would likely just be a few milliseconds, it is a possibility). In this case the process that took over consumption\n253:  * would consume from last committed offset and would repeat the insert of the last batch of data. Used in this way\n254:  * Kafka provides what is often called \"at-least-once\" delivery guarantees, as each record will likely be delivered one\n255:  * time but in failure cases could be duplicated.\n256:  * <p>\n257:  * <b>Note: Using automatic offset commits can also give you \"at-least-once\" delivery, but the requirement is that\n258:  * you must consume all data returned from each call to {@link #poll(Duration)} before any subsequent calls, or before\n259:  * {@link #close() closing} the consumer. If you fail to do either of these, it is possible for the committed offset\n260:  * to get ahead of the consumed position, which results in missing records. The advantage of using manual offset\n261:  * control is that you have direct control over when a record is considered \"consumed.\"</b>\n262:  * <p>\n263:  * The above example uses {@link #commitSync() commitSync} to mark all received records as committed. In some cases\n264:  * you may wish to have even finer control over which records have been committed by specifying an offset explicitly.\n265:  * In the example below we commit offset after we finish handling the records in each partition.\n266:  * <p>\n267:  * <pre>\n268:  * {@code\n269:  *     try {\n270:  *         while(running) {\n271:  *             ConsumerRecords<String, String> records = consumer.poll(Duration.ofMillis(Long.MAX_VALUE));\n272:  *             for (TopicPartition partition : records.partitions()) {\n273:  *                 List<ConsumerRecord<String, String>> partitionRecords = records.records(partition);\n274:  *                 for (ConsumerRecord<String, String> record : partitionRecords) {\n275:  *                     System.out.println(record.offset() + \": \" + record.value());\n276:  *                 }\n277:  *                 consumer.commitSync(Collections.singletonMap(partition, records.nextOffsets().get(partition)));\n278:  *             }\n279:  *         }\n280:  *     } finally {\n281:  *       consumer.close();\n282:  *     }\n283:  * }</pre>\n284:  *\n285:  * <b>Note: The committed offset should always be the offset of the next message that your application will read.</b>\n286:  * Thus, when calling {@link #commitSync(Map) commitSync(offsets)} you should use {@code nextRecordToBeProcessed.offset()}\n287:  * or if {@link ConsumerRecords} is exhausted already {@link ConsumerRecords#nextOffsets()} instead.\n288:  * You should also add the leader epoch as commit metadata, which can be obtained from\n289:  * {@link ConsumerRecord#leaderEpoch()} or {@link ConsumerRecords#nextOffsets()}.\n290:  *\n291:  * <h4><a name=\"manualassignment\">Manual Partition Assignment</a></h4>\n292:  *\n293:  * In the previous examples, we subscribed to the topics we were interested in and let Kafka dynamically assign a\n294:  * fair share of the partitions for those topics based on the active consumers in the group. However, in\n295:  * some cases you may need finer control over the specific partitions that are assigned. For example:\n296:  * <p>\n297:  * <ul>\n298:  * <li>If the process is maintaining some kind of local state associated with that partition (like a\n299:  * local on-disk key-value store), then it should only get records for the partition it is maintaining on disk.\n300:  * <li>If the process itself is highly available and will be restarted if it fails (perhaps using a\n301:  * cluster management framework like YARN, Mesos, or AWS facilities, or as part of a stream processing framework). In\n302:  * this case there is no need for Kafka to detect the failure and reassign the partition since the consuming process\n303:  * will be restarted on another machine.\n304:  * </ul>\n305:  * <p>\n306:  * To use this mode, instead of subscribing to the topic using {@link #subscribe(Collection) subscribe}, you just call\n307:  * {@link #assign(Collection)} with the full list of partitions that you want to consume.\n308:  *\n309:  * <pre>\n310:  * {@code\n311:  *     String topic = \"foo\";\n312:  *     TopicPartition partition0 = new TopicPartition(topic, 0);\n313:  *     TopicPartition partition1 = new TopicPartition(topic, 1);\n314:  *     consumer.assign(Arrays.asList(partition0, partition1));\n315:  * }</pre>\n316:  *\n317:  * Once assigned, you can call {@link #poll(Duration) poll} in a loop, just as in the preceding examples to consume\n318:  * records. The group that the consumer specifies is still used for committing offsets, but now the set of partitions\n319:  * will only change with another call to {@link #assign(Collection) assign}. Manual partition assignment does\n320:  * not use group coordination, so consumer failures will not cause assigned partitions to be rebalanced. Each consumer\n321:  * acts independently even if it shares a groupId with another consumer. To avoid offset commit conflicts, you should\n322:  * usually ensure that the groupId is unique for each consumer instance.\n323:  * <p>\n324:  * Note that it isn't possible to mix manual partition assignment (i.e. using {@link #assign(Collection) assign})\n325:  * with dynamic partition assignment through topic subscription (i.e. using {@link #subscribe(Collection) subscribe}).\n326:  *\n327:  * <h4><a name=\"rebalancecallback\">Storing Offsets Outside Kafka</h4>\n328:  *\n329:  * The consumer application need not use Kafka's built-in offset storage, it can store offsets in a store of its own\n330:  * choosing. The primary use case for this is allowing the application to store both the offset and the results of the\n331:  * consumption in the same system in a way that both the results and offsets are stored atomically. This is not always\n332:  * possible, but when it is it will make the consumption fully atomic and give \"exactly once\" semantics that are\n333:  * stronger than the default \"at-least once\" semantics you get with Kafka's offset commit functionality.\n334:  * <p>\n335:  * Here are a couple of examples of this type of usage:\n336:  * <ul>\n337:  * <li>If the results of the consumption are being stored in a relational database, storing the offset in the database\n338:  * as well can allow committing both the results and offset in a single transaction. Thus either the transaction will\n339:  * succeed and the offset will be updated based on what was consumed or the result will not be stored and the offset\n340:  * won't be updated.\n341:  * <li>If the results are being stored in a local store it may be possible to store the offset there as well. For\n342:  * example a search index could be built by subscribing to a particular partition and storing both the offset and the\n343:  * indexed data together. If this is done in a way that is atomic, it is often possible to have it be the case that even\n344:  * if a crash occurs that causes unsync'd data to be lost, whatever is left has the corresponding offset stored as well.\n345:  * This means that in this case the indexing process that comes back having lost recent updates just resumes indexing\n346:  * from what it has ensuring that no updates are lost.\n347:  * </ul>\n348:  * <p>\n349:  * Each record comes with its own offset, so to manage your own offset you just need to do the following:\n350:  *\n351:  * <ul>\n352:  * <li>Configure <code>enable.auto.commit=false</code>\n353:  * <li>Use the offset provided with each {@link ConsumerRecord} to save your position.\n354:  * <li>On restart restore the position of the consumer using {@link #seek(TopicPartition, long)}.\n355:  * </ul>\n356:  *\n357:  * <p>\n358:  * This type of usage is simplest when the partition assignment is also done manually (this would be likely in the\n359:  * search index use case described above). If the partition assignment is done automatically special care is\n360:  * needed to handle the case where partition assignments change. This can be done by providing a\n361:  * {@link ConsumerRebalanceListener} instance in the call to {@link #subscribe(Collection, ConsumerRebalanceListener)}\n362:  * and {@link #subscribe(Pattern, ConsumerRebalanceListener)}.\n363:  * For example, when partitions are taken from a consumer the consumer will want to commit its offset for those partitions by\n364:  * implementing {@link ConsumerRebalanceListener#onPartitionsRevoked(Collection)}. When partitions are assigned to a\n365:  * consumer, the consumer will want to look up the offset for those new partitions and correctly initialize the consumer\n366:  * to that position by implementing {@link ConsumerRebalanceListener#onPartitionsAssigned(Collection)}.\n367:  * <p>\n368:  * Another common use for {@link ConsumerRebalanceListener} is to flush any caches the application maintains for\n369:  * partitions that are moved elsewhere.\n370:  *\n371:  * <h4>Controlling The Consumer's Position</h4>\n372:  *\n373:  * In most use cases the consumer will simply consume records from beginning to end, periodically committing its\n374:  * position (either automatically or manually). However Kafka allows the consumer to manually control its position,\n375:  * moving forward or backwards in a partition at will. This means a consumer can re-consume older records, or skip to\n376:  * the most recent records without actually consuming the intermediate records.\n377:  * <p>\n378:  * There are several instances where manually controlling the consumer's position can be useful.\n379:  * <p>\n380:  * One case is for time-sensitive record processing it may make sense for a consumer that falls far enough behind to not\n381:  * attempt to catch up processing all records, but rather just skip to the most recent records.\n382:  * <p>\n383:  * Another use case is for a system that maintains local state as described in the previous section. In such a system\n384:  * the consumer will want to initialize its position on start-up to whatever is contained in the local store. Likewise\n385:  * if the local state is destroyed (say because the disk is lost) the state may be recreated on a new machine by\n386:  * re-consuming all the data and recreating the state (assuming that Kafka is retaining sufficient history).\n387:  * <p>\n388:  * Kafka allows specifying the position using {@link #seek(TopicPartition, long)} to specify the new position. Special\n389:  * methods for seeking to the earliest and latest offset the server maintains are also available (\n390:  * {@link #seekToBeginning(Collection)} and {@link #seekToEnd(Collection)} respectively).\n391:  *\n392:  * <h4>Consumption Flow Control</h4>\n393:  *\n394:  * If a consumer is assigned multiple partitions to fetch data from, it will try to consume from all of them at the same time,\n395:  * effectively giving these partitions the same priority for consumption. However in some cases consumers may want to\n396:  * first focus on fetching from some subset of the assigned partitions at full speed, and only start fetching other partitions\n397:  * when these partitions have few or no data to consume.\n398:  *\n399:  * <p>\n400:  * One of such cases is stream processing, where processor fetches from two topics and performs the join on these two streams.\n401:  * When one of the topics is long lagging behind the other, the processor would like to pause fetching from the ahead topic\n402:  * in order to get the lagging stream to catch up. Another example is bootstrapping upon consumer starting up where there are\n403:  * a lot of history data to catch up, the applications usually want to get the latest data on some of the topics before consider\n404:  * fetching other topics.\n405:  *\n406:  * <p>\n407:  * Kafka supports dynamic controlling of consumption flows by using {@link #pause(Collection)} and {@link #resume(Collection)}\n408:  * to pause the consumption on the specified assigned partitions and resume the consumption\n409:  * on the specified paused partitions respectively in the future {@link #poll(Duration)} calls.\n410:  *\n411:  * <h3>Reading Transactional Messages</h3>\n412:  *\n413:  * <p>\n414:  * Transactions were introduced in Kafka 0.11.0 wherein applications can write to multiple topics and partitions atomically.\n415:  * In order for this to work, consumers reading from these partitions should be configured to only read committed data.\n416:  * This can be achieved by setting the {@code isolation.level=read_committed} in the consumer's configuration.\n417:  *\n418:  * <p>\n419:  * In <code>read_committed</code> mode, the consumer will read only those transactional messages which have been\n420:  * successfully committed. It will continue to read non-transactional messages as before. There is no client-side\n421:  * buffering in <code>read_committed</code> mode. Instead, the end offset of a partition for a <code>read_committed</code>\n422:  * consumer would be the offset of the first message in the partition belonging to an open transaction. This offset\n423:  * is known as the 'Last Stable Offset'(LSO).</p>\n424:  *\n425:  * <p>\n426:  * A {@code read_committed} consumer will only read up to the LSO and filter out any transactional\n427:  * messages which have been aborted. The LSO also affects the behavior of {@link #seekToEnd(Collection)} and\n428:  * {@link #endOffsets(Collection)} for {@code read_committed} consumers, details of which are in each method's documentation.\n429:  * Finally, the fetch lag metrics are also adjusted to be relative to the LSO for {@code read_committed} consumers.\n430:  *\n431:  * <p>\n432:  * Partitions with transactional messages will include commit or abort markers which indicate the result of a transaction.\n433:  * There markers are not returned to applications, yet have an offset in the log. As a result, applications reading from\n434:  * topics with transactional messages will see gaps in the consumed offsets. These missing messages would be the transaction\n435:  * markers, and they are filtered out for consumers in both isolation levels. Additionally, applications using\n436:  * {@code read_committed} consumers may also see gaps due to aborted transactions, since those messages would not\n437:  * be returned by the consumer and yet would have valid offsets.\n438:  *\n439:  * <h3><a name=\"multithreaded\">Multi-threaded Processing</a></h3>\n440:  *\n441:  * The Kafka consumer is NOT thread-safe. It is the responsibility of the user to ensure that multi-threaded access\n442:  * is properly synchronized. Un-synchronized access will result in {@link ConcurrentModificationException}.\n443:  *\n444:  * <p>\n445:  * The only exception to this rule is {@link #wakeup()}, which can safely be used from an external thread to\n446:  * interrupt an active operation. In this case, a {@link org.apache.kafka.common.errors.WakeupException} will be\n447:  * thrown from the thread blocking on the operation. This can be used to shutdown the consumer from another thread.\n448:  * The following snippet shows the typical pattern:\n449:  *\n450:  * <pre>\n451:  * {@code\n452:  * public class KafkaConsumerRunner implements Runnable {\n453:  *     private final AtomicBoolean closed = new AtomicBoolean(false);\n454:  *     private final KafkaConsumer consumer;\n455:  *\n456:  *     public KafkaConsumerRunner(KafkaConsumer consumer) {\n457:  *       this.consumer = consumer;\n458:  *     }\n459:  *\n460:  *     @Override\n461:  *     public void run() {\n462:  *         try {\n463:  *             consumer.subscribe(Arrays.asList(\"topic\"));\n464:  *             while (!closed.get()) {\n465:  *                 ConsumerRecords records = consumer.poll(Duration.ofMillis(10000));\n466:  *                 // Handle new records\n467:  *             }\n468:  *         } catch (WakeupException e) {\n469:  *             // Ignore exception if closing\n470:  *             if (!closed.get()) throw e;\n471:  *         } finally {\n472:  *             consumer.close();\n473:  *         }\n474:  *     }\n475:  *\n476:  *     // Shutdown hook which can be called from a separate thread\n477:  *     public void shutdown() {\n478:  *         closed.set(true);\n479:  *         consumer.wakeup();\n480:  *     }\n481:  * }\n482:  * }</pre>\n483:  *\n484:  * Then in a separate thread, the consumer can be shutdown by setting the closed flag and waking up the consumer.\n485:  *\n486:  * <p>\n487:  * <pre>\n488:  * {@code\n489:  *     closed.set(true);\n490:  *     consumer.wakeup();\n491:  * }</pre>\n492:  *\n493:  * <p>\n494:  * Note that while it is possible to use thread interrupts instead of {@link #wakeup()} to abort a blocking operation\n495:  * (in which case, {@link InterruptException} will be raised), we discourage their use since they may cause a clean\n496:  * shutdown of the consumer to be aborted. Interrupts are mainly supported for those cases where using {@link #wakeup()}\n497:  * is impossible, e.g. when a consumer thread is managed by code that is unaware of the Kafka client.\n498:  *\n499:  * <p>\n500:  * We have intentionally avoided implementing a particular threading model for processing. This leaves several\n501:  * options for implementing multi-threaded processing of records.\n502:  *\n503:  * <h4>1. One Consumer Per Thread</h4>\n504:  *\n505:  * A simple option is to give each thread its own consumer instance. Here are the pros and cons of this approach:\n506:  * <ul>\n507:  * <li><b>PRO</b>: It is the easiest to implement\n508:  * <li><b>PRO</b>: It is often the fastest as no inter-thread co-ordination is needed\n509:  * <li><b>PRO</b>: It makes in-order processing on a per-partition basis very easy to implement (each thread just\n510:  * processes messages in the order it receives them).\n511:  * <li><b>CON</b>: More consumers means more TCP connections to the cluster (one per thread). In general Kafka handles\n512:  * connections very efficiently so this is generally a small cost.\n513:  * <li><b>CON</b>: Multiple consumers means more requests being sent to the server and slightly less batching of data\n514:  * which can cause some drop in I/O throughput.\n515:  * <li><b>CON</b>: The number of total threads across all processes will be limited by the total number of partitions.\n516:  * </ul>\n517:  *\n518:  * <h4>2. Decouple Consumption and Processing</h4>\n519:  *\n520:  * Another alternative is to have one or more consumer threads that do all data consumption and hands off\n521:  * {@link ConsumerRecords} instances to a blocking queue consumed by a pool of processor threads that actually handle\n522:  * the record processing.\n523:  *\n524:  * This option likewise has pros and cons:\n525:  * <ul>\n526:  * <li><b>PRO</b>: This option allows independently scaling the number of consumers and processors. This makes it\n527:  * possible to have a single consumer that feeds many processor threads, avoiding any limitation on partitions.\n528:  * <li><b>CON</b>: Guaranteeing order across the processors requires particular care as the threads will execute\n529:  * independently an earlier chunk of data may actually be processed after a later chunk of data just due to the luck of\n530:  * thread execution timing. For processing that has no ordering requirements this is not a problem.\n531:  * <li><b>CON</b>: Manually committing the position becomes harder as it requires that all threads co-ordinate to ensure\n532:  * that processing is complete for that partition.\n533:  * </ul>\n534:  *\n535:  * There are many possible variations on this approach. For example each processor thread can have its own queue, and\n536:  * the consumer threads can hash into these queues using the TopicPartition to ensure in-order consumption and simplify\n537:  * commit.\n538:  */\n539: public class KafkaConsumer<K, V> implements Consumer<K, V> {\n540: \n541:     private static final ConsumerDelegateCreator CREATOR = new ConsumerDelegateCreator();\n542: \n543:     private final ConsumerDelegate<K, V> delegate;\n544: \n545:     /**\n546:      * A consumer is instantiated by providing a set of key-value pairs as configuration. Valid configuration strings\n547:      * are documented <a href=\"http://kafka.apache.org/documentation.html#consumerconfigs\" >here</a>. Values can be\n548:      * either strings or objects of the appropriate type (for example a numeric configuration would accept either the\n549:      * string \"42\" or the integer 42).\n550:      * <p>\n551:      * Valid configuration strings are documented at {@link ConsumerConfig}.\n552:      * <p>\n553:      * Note: after creating a {@code KafkaConsumer} you must always {@link #close()} it to avoid resource leaks.\n554:      *\n555:      * @param configs The consumer configs\n556:      */\n557:     public KafkaConsumer(Map<String, Object> configs) {\n558:         this(configs, null, null);\n559:     }\n560: \n561:     /**\n562:      * A consumer is instantiated by providing a {@link java.util.Properties} object as configuration.\n563:      * <p>\n564:      * Valid configuration strings are documented at {@link ConsumerConfig}.\n565:      * <p>\n566:      * Note: after creating a {@code KafkaConsumer} you must always {@link #close()} it to avoid resource leaks.\n567:      *\n568:      * @param properties The consumer configuration properties\n569:      */\n570:     public KafkaConsumer(Properties properties) {\n571:         this(properties, null, null);\n572:     }\n573: \n574:     /**\n575:      * A consumer is instantiated by providing a {@link java.util.Properties} object as configuration, and a\n576:      * key and a value {@link Deserializer}.\n577:      * <p>\n578:      * Valid configuration strings are documented at {@link ConsumerConfig}.\n579:      * <p>\n580:      * Note: after creating a {@code KafkaConsumer} you must always {@link #close()} it to avoid resource leaks.\n581:      *\n582:      * @param properties The consumer configuration properties\n583:      * @param keyDeserializer The deserializer for key that implements {@link Deserializer}. The configure() method\n603:      *            won't be called in the consumer when the deserializer is passed in directly.\n604:      * @param valueDeserializer The deserializer for value that implements {@link Deserializer}. The configure() method\n605:      *            won't be called in the consumer when the deserializer is passed in directly.\n606:      */\n607:     public KafkaConsumer(Map<String, Object> configs,\n608:                          Deserializer<K> keyDeserializer,\n609:                          Deserializer<V> valueDeserializer) {\n610:         this(new ConsumerConfig(ConsumerConfig.appendDeserializerToConfig(configs, keyDeserializer, valueDeserializer)),\n611:                 keyDeserializer, valueDeserializer);\n612:     }\n613: \n614:     KafkaConsumer(ConsumerConfig config, Deserializer<K> keyDeserializer, Deserializer<V> valueDeserializer) {\n615:         delegate = CREATOR.create(config, keyDeserializer, valueDeserializer);\n616:     }\n617: \n618:     KafkaConsumer(LogContext logContext,\n619:                   Time time,\n620:                   ConsumerConfig config,\n621:                   Deserializer<K> keyDeserializer,\n622:                   Deserializer<V> valueDeserializer,\n623:                   KafkaClient client,\n624:                   SubscriptionState subscriptions,\n625:                   ConsumerMetadata metadata,\n626:                   List<ConsumerPartitionAssignor> assignors) {\n627:         delegate = CREATOR.create(\n628:             logContext,\n629:             time,\n630:             config,\n631:             keyDeserializer,\n632:             valueDeserializer,\n633:             client,\n634:             subscriptions,\n635:             metadata,\n636:             assignors\n637:         );\n638:     }\n639: \n640:     /**\n641:      * Get the set of partitions currently assigned to this consumer. This list will replace the current\n664:      * assignment (if there is one).</b> Note that it is not possible to combine topic subscription with group management\n665:      * with manual partition assignment through {@link #assign(Collection)}.\n666:      *\n667:      * If the given list of topics is empty, it is treated the same as {@link #unsubscribe()}.\n668:      *\n669:      * <p>\n670:      * As part of group management, the group coordinator will keep track of the list of consumers that belong to a particular\n671:      * group and will trigger a rebalance operation if any one of the following events are triggered:\n672:      * <ul>\n673:      * <li>Number of partitions change for any of the subscribed topics\n674:      * <li>A subscribed topic is created or deleted\n675:      * <li>An existing member of the consumer group is shutdown or fails\n676:      * <li>A new member is added to the consumer group\n677:      * </ul>\n678:      * <p>\n679:      * When any of these events are triggered, the provided listener will be invoked in this way:\n680:      * <ul>\n681:      *     <li>{@link ConsumerRebalanceListener#onPartitionsRevoked(Collection)} will be invoked with the partitions to revoke, before re-assigning those partitions to another consumer.</li>\n682:      *     <li>{@link ConsumerRebalanceListener#onPartitionsAssigned(Collection)} will be invoked when the rebalance completes (even if no new partitions are assigned to the consumer)</li>\n683:      * </ul>\n684:      * Note that rebalances will only occur during an active call to {@link #poll(Duration)}, so callbacks will\n685:      * also only be invoked during that time.\n686:      *\n687:      * The provided listener will immediately override any listener set in a previous call to subscribe.\n688:      * It is guaranteed, however, that the partitions revoked/assigned through this interface are from topics\n689:      * subscribed in this call. See {@link ConsumerRebalanceListener} for more details.\n690:      *\n691:      * @param topics The list of topics to subscribe to\n692:      * @param listener Non-null listener instance to get notifications on partition assignment/revocation for the\n693:      *                 subscribed topics\n694:      * @throws IllegalArgumentException If topics is null or contains null or empty elements, or if listener is null\n695:      * @throws IllegalStateException If {@code subscribe()} is called previously with pattern, or assign is called\n696:      *                               previously (without a subsequent call to {@link #unsubscribe()}), or if not\n697:      *                               configured at-least one partition assignment strategy\n698:      */\n699:     @Override\n700:     public void subscribe(Collection<String> topics, ConsumerRebalanceListener listener) {\n701:         delegate.subscribe(topics, listener);\n702:     }\n703: \n704:     /**\n705:      * Subscribe to the given list of topics to get dynamically assigned partitions.\n706:      * <b>Topic subscriptions are not incremental. This list will replace the current\n707:      * assignment (if there is one).</b> It is not possible to combine topic subscription with group management\n708:      * with manual partition assignment through {@link #assign(Collection)}.\n709:      *\n710:      * If the given list of topics is empty, it is treated the same as {@link #unsubscribe()}.\n711:      *\n712:      * <p>\n713:      * This is a short-hand for {@link #subscribe(Collection, ConsumerRebalanceListener)}, which\n714:      * uses a no-op listener. If you need the ability to seek to particular offsets, you should prefer\n715:      * {@link #subscribe(Collection, ConsumerRebalanceListener)}, since group rebalances will cause partition offsets\n716:      * to be reset. You should also provide your own listener if you are doing your own offset\n717:      * management since the listener gives you an opportunity to commit offsets before a rebalance finishes.\n718:      *\n719:      * @param topics The list of topics to subscribe to\n720:      * @throws IllegalArgumentException If topics is null or contains null or empty elements\n721:      * @throws IllegalStateException If {@code subscribe()} is called previously with pattern, or assign is called\n722:      *                               previously (without a subsequent call to {@link #unsubscribe()}), or if not\n723:      *                               configured at-least one partition assignment strategy\n724:      */\n725:     @Override\n726:     public void subscribe(Collection<String> topics) {\n727:         delegate.subscribe(topics);\n728:     }\n729: \n730:     /**\n731:      * Subscribe to all topics matching specified pattern to get dynamically assigned partitions.\n732:      * The pattern matching will be done periodically against all topics existing at the time of check.\n733:      * This can be controlled through the {@code metadata.max.age.ms} configuration: by lowering\n734:      * the max metadata age, the consumer will refresh metadata more often and check for matching topics.\n735:      * <p>\n736:      * See {@link #subscribe(Collection, ConsumerRebalanceListener)} for details on the\n737:      * use of the {@link ConsumerRebalanceListener}. This is only supported under the\n803:      * CONSUMER group protocol (see {@link ConsumerConfig#GROUP_PROTOCOL_CONFIG})\n804:      * <p>\n805:      * If the provided pattern is not compatible with Google RE2/J, an {@link InvalidRegularExpression} will be\n806:      * eventually thrown on a call to {@link #poll(Duration)} following this call to subscribe.\n807:      * <p>\n808:      * This is a short-hand for {@link #subscribe(Pattern, ConsumerRebalanceListener)}, which\n809:      * uses a no-op listener. The last\n863:      * consumed offset can be manually set through {@link #seek(TopicPartition, long)} or automatically set as the last committed\n864:      * offset for the subscribed list of partitions\n865:      *\n866:      * <p>\n867:      * This method returns immediately if there are records available or if the position advances past control records\n868:      * or aborted transactions when isolation.level=read_committed.\n869:      * Otherwise, it will await the passed timeout. If the timeout expires, an empty record set will be returned.\n870:      * Note that this method may block beyond the timeout in order to execute custom\n871:      * {@link ConsumerRebalanceListener} callbacks.\n872:      *\n873:      *\n874:      * @param timeout The maximum time to block (must not be greater than {@link Long#MAX_VALUE} milliseconds)\n875:      *\n876:      * @return map of topic to records since the last fetch for the subscribed list of topics and partitions\n877:      *\n878:      * @throws org.apache.kafka.clients.consumer.InvalidOffsetException if the offset for a partition or set of\n879:      *             partitions is undefined or out of range and no offset reset policy has been configured\n880:      * @throws org.apache.kafka.common.errors.WakeupException if {@link #wakeup()} is called before or while this\n881:      *             function is called\n882:      * @throws org.apache.kafka.common.errors.InterruptException if the calling thread is interrupted before or while\n883:      *             this function is called\n884:      * @throws org.apache.kafka.common.errors.AuthenticationException if authentication fails. See the exception for more details\n885:      * @throws org.apache.kafka.common.errors.AuthorizationException if caller lacks Read access to any of the subscribed\n886:      *             topics or to the configured groupId. See the exception for more details\n887:      * @throws org.apache.kafka.common.KafkaException for any other unrecoverable errors (e.g. invalid groupId or\n888:      *             session timeout, errors deserializing key/value pairs, your rebalance callback thrown exceptions,\n889:      *             or any new error cases in future versions)\n890:      * @throws java.lang.IllegalArgumentException if the timeout value is negative\n891:      * @throws java.lang.IllegalStateException if the consumer is not subscribed to any topics or manually assigned any\n892:      *             partitions to consume from\n893:      * @throws java.lang.ArithmeticException if the timeout is greater than {@link Long#MAX_VALUE} milliseconds.\n894:      * @throws org.apache.kafka.common.errors.InvalidTopicException if the current subscription contains any invalid\n895:      *             topic (per {@link org.apache.kafka.common.internals.Topic#validate(String)})\n896:      * @throws org.apache.kafka.common.errors.UnsupportedVersionException if the consumer attempts to fetch stable offsets\n897:      *             when the broker doesn't support this feature. The offsets committed using this API will be used on the first fetch after\n913:      * every rebalance and also on startup. As such, if you need to store offsets in anything other than Kafka, this API\n914:      * should not be used.\n915:      * <p>\n916:      * This is a synchronous commit and will block until either the commit succeeds, an unrecoverable error is\n917:      * encountered (in which case it is thrown to the caller), or the timeout specified by {@code default.api.timeout.ms} expires\n918:      * (in which case a {@link org.apache.kafka.common.errors.TimeoutException} is thrown to the caller).\n919:      * <p>\n920:      * Note that asynchronous offset commits sent previously with the {@link #commitAsync(OffsetCommitCallback)}\n921:      * (or similar) are guaranteed to have their callbacks invoked prior to completion of this method,\n922:      * but only when the consumer is using the consumer group protocol.\n923:      *\n924:      * @throws org.apache.kafka.clients.consumer.CommitFailedException if the commit failed and cannot be retried.\n925:      *             This fatal error can only occur if you are using automatic group management with {@link #subscribe(Collection)},\n926:      *             or if there is an active group with the same <code>group.id</code> which is using group management. The offsets committed using this API will be used on the first fetch after\n959:      * every rebalance and also on startup. As such, if you need to store offsets in anything other than Kafka, this API\n960:      * should not be used.\n961:      * <p>\n962:      * This is a synchronous commit and will block until either the commit succeeds, an unrecoverable error is\n963:      * encountered (in which case it is thrown to the caller), or the passed timeout expires.\n964:      * <p>\n965:      * Note that asynchronous offset commits sent previously with the {@link #commitAsync(OffsetCommitCallback)}\n966:      * (or similar) are guaranteed to have their callbacks invoked prior to completion of this method,\n967:      * but only when the consumer is using the consumer group protocol.\n968:      *\n969:      * @throws org.apache.kafka.clients.consumer.CommitFailedException if the commit failed and cannot be retried.\n970:      *             This can only occur if you are using automatic group management with {@link #subscribe(Collection)},\n971:      *             or if there is an active group with the same <code>group.id</code> which is using group management. Even if a larger timeout is specified, the consumer will not wait longer than\n1813:      * {@link ConsumerConfig#REQUEST_TIMEOUT_MS_CONFIG} for these requests to complete during the close operation.\n1814:      * Note that the execution time of callbacks (such as {@link OffsetCommitCallback} and\n1815:      * {@link ConsumerRebalanceListener}) does not consume time from the close timeout.\n1816:      * <p>\n1817:      * This close operation will attempt all shutdown steps even if one of them fails.\n1818:      * It logs all encountered errors, continues to execute the next steps, and finally throws the first error found.\n1819:      *\n1820:      * @param timeout The maximum time to wait for consumer to close gracefully. The value must be\n1821:      *                non-negative. Specifying a timeout of zero means do not wait for pending requests to complete.\n1822:      * @throws IllegalArgumentException If the {@code timeout} is negative.\n1823:      * @throws WakeupException          if {@link #wakeup()} is called before or while this function is called\n1824:      * @throws InterruptException       if the calling thread is interrupted before or while this function is called\n1825:      * @throws KafkaException           for any other error during close\n1826:      *                                  (e.g., errors thrown from rebalance callbacks or commit callbacks from previous asynchronous commits)\n1827:      */\n1828:     @Deprecated(since = \"4.1\")\n1829:     @Override\n1830:     public void close(Duration timeout) {\n1831:         delegate.close(timeout);\n1832:     }\n1833: \n1834:     /**\n1835:      * Close the consumer cleanly. {@link CloseOptions} allows to specify a timeout and a\n1836:      * {@link CloseOptions.GroupMembershipOperation leave group behavior}.\n1837:      * If no timeout is specified, the default timeout of 30 seconds is used.\n1838:      * If no leave group behavior is specified, the {@link CloseOptions.GroupMembershipOperation#DEFAULT default\n1839:      * leave group behavior} is used.\n1840:      * <p>\n1841:      * This method waits up to the timeout for the consumer to complete pending commits and maybe leave the group,\n1842:      * depending on the specified leave group behavior.\n1843:      * If auto-commit is enabled, this will commit the current offsets if possible within the\n1844:      * timeout. If the consumer is unable to complete offset commits and to gracefully leave the group (if applicable)\n1845:      * before the timeout expires, the consumer is force closed. Note that {@link #wakeup()} cannot be\n1846:      * used to interrupt close.\n1847:      * <p>\n1848:      * The actual maximum wait time is bounded by the {@link ConsumerConfig#REQUEST_TIMEOUT_MS_CONFIG} setting, which\n1849:      * only applies to operations performed with the broker (coordinator-related requests and\n1850:      * fetch sessions). Even if a larger timeout is specified, the consumer will not wait longer than\n1851:      * {@link ConsumerConfig#REQUEST_TIMEOUT_MS_CONFIG} for these requests to complete during the close operation.\n1852:      * Note that the execution time of callbacks (such as {@link OffsetCommitCallback} and\n1853:      * {@link ConsumerRebalanceListener}) does not consume time from the close timeout.\n1854:      * <p>\n1855:      * This close operation will attempt all shutdown steps even if one of them fails.\n1856:      * It logs all encountered errors, continues to execute the next steps, and finally throws the first error found.\n1857:      *\n1858:      * @param option see {@link CloseOptions}; cannot be {@code null}\n1859:      * @throws IllegalArgumentException If the {@code option} timeout is negative\n1860:      * @throws WakeupException          if {@link #wakeup()} is called before or while this function is called\n1861:      * @throws InterruptException       if the calling thread is interrupted before or while this function is called\n1862:      * @throws KafkaException           for any other error during close\n1863:      *                                  (e.g., errors thrown from rebalance callbacks or commit callbacks from previous asynchronous commits)\n1864:      */\n1865:     @Override\n1866:     public void close(CloseOptions option) {\n1867:         delegate.close(option);\n1868:     }\n1869: \n1870:     /**\n1871:      * Wakeup the consumer. This method is thread-safe and is useful in particular to abort a long poll.\n1872:      * The thread which is blocking in an operation will throw {@link org.apache.kafka.common.errors.WakeupException}.\n1873:      * If no thread is blocking in a method which can throw {@link org.apache.kafka.common.errors.WakeupException}, the next call to such a method will raise it instead.\n1874:      */\n1875:     @Override\n1876:     public void wakeup() {\n1877:         delegate.wakeup();\n1878:     }\n1879: \n1880:     // Functions below are for testing only\n1881:     String clientId() {\n1882:         return delegate.clientId();\n1883:     }\n1884: \n1885:     Metrics metricsRegistry() {\n1886:         return delegate.metricsRegistry();\n1887:     }\n1888: \n1889:     KafkaConsumerMetrics kafkaConsumerMetrics() {\n1890:         return delegate.kafkaConsumerMetrics();\n1891:     }\n1892: \n1893:     boolean updateAssignmentMetadataIfNeeded(final Timer timer) {\n1894:         return delegate.updateAssignmentMetadataIfNeeded(timer);\n1895:     }\n1896: }\n1897: ","url":"https://sourcegraph.sourcegraph.com/github.com/sg-evals/kafka--e678b4b@47ac12bd6042edf23f1dbca3d789db945d62802d/-/blob/clients/src/main/java/org/apache/kafka/clients/consumer/KafkaConsumer.java"}}}

org.apache.kafka.common.utils.LogContext;\\n38: import org.apache.kafka.common.utils.Time;\\n39: import org.apache.kafka.common.utils.Timer;\\n40: \\n41: import java.time.Duration;\\n42: import java.util.Collection;\\n43: import java.util.ConcurrentModificationException;\\n44: import java.util.List;\\n45: import java.util.Map;\\n46: import java.util.OptionalLong;\\n47: import java.util.Properties;\\n48: import java.util.Set;\\n49: import java.util.regex.Pattern;\\n50: \\n51: import static org.apache.kafka.common.utils.Utils.propsToMap;\\n52: \\n53: /**\\n54:  * A client that consumes records from a Kafka cluster.\\n55:  * <p>\\n56:  * This client transparently handles the failure of Kafka brokers, and transparently adapts as topic partitions\\n57:  * it fetches migrate within the cluster. This client also interacts with the broker to allow groups of\\n58:  * consumers to load balance consumption using <a href=\\\"#consumergroups\\\">consumer groups</a>.\\n59:  * <p>\\n60:  * The consumer maintains TCP connections to the necessary brokers to fetch data.\\n61:  * Failure to close the consumer after use will leak these connections.\\n62:  * The consumer is not thread-safe. See <a href=\\\"#multithreaded\\\">Multi-threaded Processing</a> for more details.\\n63:  *\\n64:  * <h3>Cross-Version Compatibility</h3>\\n65:  * This client can communicate with brokers that are version 0.10.0 or newer. Older or newer brokers may not support\\n66:  * certain features. For example, 0.10.0 brokers do not support offsetsForTimes, because this feature was added\\n67:  * in version 0.10.1. You will receive an {@link org.apache.kafka.common.errors.UnsupportedVersionException}\\n68:  * when invoking an API that is not available on the running broker version.\\n69:  * <p>\\n70:  *\\n71:  * <h3>Offsets and Consumer Position</h3>\\n72:  * Kafka maintains a numerical offset for each record in a partition. This offset acts as a unique identifier of\\n73:  * a record within that partition, and also denotes the position of the consumer in the partition. For example, a consumer\\n74:  * which is at position 5 has consumed records with offsets 0 through 4 and will next receive the record with offset 5.\\n75:  * Note that offsets are not guaranteed to be consecutive (such as compacted topic or when records have been produced\\n76:  * using transactions). For example, if the consumer did read a record with offset 4, but 5 is not an offset\\n77:  * with a record, its position might advance to 6 (or higher) directly. Similarly, if the consumer's position is 5,\\n78:  * but there is no record with offset 5, the consumer will return the record with the next higher offset.\\n79:  * There are actually two notions of position relevant to the user of the consumer:\\n80:  * <p>\\n81:  * The {@link #position(TopicPartition) position} of the consumer gives the offset of the next record that will be given\\n82:  * out. It will be one larger than the highest offset the consumer has seen in that partition. It automatically advances\\n83:  * every time the consumer receives messages in a call to {@link #poll(Duration)}.\\n84:  * <p>\\n85:  * The {@link #commitSync() committed position} is the last offset that has been stored securely. Should the\\n86:  * process fail and restart, this is the offset that the consumer will recover to. The consumer can either automatically commit\\n87:  * offsets periodically; or it can choose to control this committed position manually by calling one of the commit APIs\\n88:  * (e.g. {@link #commitSync() commitSync} and {@link #commitAsync(OffsetCommitCallback) commitAsync}).\\n89:  * <p>\\n90:  * This distinction gives the consumer control over when a record is considered consumed. It is discussed in further\\n91:  * detail below.\\n92:  *\\n93:  * <h3><a name=\\\"consumergroups\\\">Consumer Groups and Topic Subscriptions</a></h3>\\n94:  *\\n95:  * Kafka uses the concept of <i>consumer groups</i> to allow a pool of processes to divide the work of consuming and\\n96:  * processing records. These processes can either be running on the same machine or they can be\\n97:  * distributed over many machines to provide scalability and fault tolerance for processing. All consumer instances\\n98:  * sharing the same {@code group.id} will be part of the same consumer group.\\n99:  * <p>\\n100:  * Each consumer in a group can dynamically set the list of topics it wants to subscribe to through one of the\\n101:  * {@link #subscribe(Collection, ConsumerRebalanceListener) subscribe} APIs. Kafka will deliver each message in the\\n102:  * subscribed topics to one process in each consumer group. This is achieved by balancing the partitions between all\\n103:  * members in the consumer group so that each partition is assigned to exactly one consumer in the group. So if there\\n104:  * is a topic with four partitions, and a consumer group with two processes, each process would consume from two partitions.\\n105:  * <p>\\n106:  * Membership in a consumer group is maintained dynamically: if a process fails, the partitions assigned to it will\\n107:  * be reassigned to other consumers in the same group. Similarly, if a new consumer joins the group, partitions will be moved\\n108:  * from existing consumers to the new one. This is known as <i>rebalancing</i> the group and is discussed in more\\n109:  * detail <a href=\\\"#failuredetection\\\">below</a>. Group rebalancing is also used when new partitions are added\\n110:  * to one of the subscribed topics or when a new topic matching a {@link #subscribe(Pattern, ConsumerRebalanceListener) subscribed regex}\\n111:  * is created. The group will automatically detect the new partitions through periodic metadata refreshes and\\n112:  * assign them to members of the group.\\n113:  * <p>\\n114:  * Conceptually you can think of a consumer group as being a single logical subscriber that happens to be made up of\\n115:  * multiple processes. As a multi-subscriber system, Kafka naturally supports having any number of consumer groups for a\\n116:  * given topic without duplicating data (additional consumers are actually quite cheap).\\n117:  * <p>\\n118:  * This is a slight generalization of the functionality that is common in messaging systems. To get semantics similar to\\n119:  * a queue in a traditional messaging system all processes would be part of a single consumer group and hence record\\n120:  * delivery would be balanced over the group like with a queue. Unlike a traditional messaging system, though, you can\\n121:  * have multiple such groups. To get semantics similar to pub-sub in a traditional messaging system each process would\\n122:  * have its own consumer group, so each process would subscribe to all the records published to the topic.\\n123:  * <p>\\n124:  * In addition, when group reassignment happens automatically, consumers can be notified through a {@link ConsumerRebalanceListener},\\n125:  * which allows them to finish necessary application-level logic such as state cleanup, manual offset\\n126:  * commits, etc. See <a href=\\\"#rebalancecallback\\\">Storing Offsets Outside Kafka</a> for more details.\\n127:  * <p>\\n128:  * It is also possible for the consumer to <a href=\\\"#manualassignment\\\">manually assign</a> specific partitions\\n129:  * (similar to the older \\\"simple\\\" consumer) using {@link #assign(Collection)}. In this case, dynamic partition\\n130:  * assignment and consumer group coordination will be disabled.\\n131:  *\\n132:  * <h3><a name=\\\"failuredetection\\\">Detecting Consumer Failures</a></h3>\\n133:  *\\n134:  * After subscribing to a set of topics, the consumer will automatically join the group when {@link #poll(Duration)} is\\n135:  * invoked. The poll API is designed to ensure consumer liveness. As long as you continue to call poll, the consumer\\n136:  * will stay in the group and continue to receive messages from the partitions it was assigned. Underneath the covers,\\n137:  * the consumer sends periodic heartbeats to the server. If the consumer crashes or is unable to send heartbeats for\\n138:  * a duration of {@code session.timeout.ms}, then the consumer will be considered dead and its partitions will\\n139:  * be reassigned.\\n140:  * <p>\\n141:  * It is also possible that the consumer could encounter a \\\"livelock\\\" situation where it is continuing\\n142:  * to send heartbeats, but no progress is being made. To prevent the consumer from holding onto its partitions\\n143:  * indefinitely in this case, we provide a liveness detection mechanism using the {@code max.poll.interval.ms}\\n144:  * setting. Basically if you don't call poll at least as frequently as the configured max interval,\\n145:  * then the client will proactively leave the group so that another consumer can take over its partitions. When this happens,\\n146:  * you may see an offset commit failure (as indicated by a {@link CommitFailedException} thrown from a call to {@link #commitSync()}).\\n147:  * This is a safety mechanism which guarantees that only active members of the group are able to commit offsets.\\n148:  * So to stay in the group, you must continue to call poll.\\n149:  * <p>\\n150:  * The consumer provides two configuration settings to control the behavior of the poll loop:\\n151:  * <ol>\\n152:  *     <li><code>max.poll.interval.ms</code>: By increasing the interval between expected polls, you can give\\n153:  *     the consumer more time to handle a batch of records returned from {@link #poll(Duration)}. The drawback\\n154:  *     is that increasing this value may delay a group rebalance since the consumer will only join the rebalance\\n155:  *     inside the call to poll. You can use this setting to bound the time to finish a rebalance, but\\n156:  *     you risk slower progress if the consumer cannot actually call {@link #poll(Duration) poll} often enough.</li>\\n157:  *     <li><code>max.poll.records</code>: Use this setting to limit the total records returned from a single\\n158:  *     call to poll. This can make it easier to predict the maximum that must be handled within each poll\\n159:  *     interval. By tuning this value, you may be able to reduce the poll interval, which will reduce the\\n160:  *     impact of group rebalancing.</li>\\n161:  * </ol>\\n162:  * <p>\\n163:  * For use cases where message processing time varies unpredictably, neither of these options may be sufficient.\\n164:  * The recommended way to handle these cases is to move message processing to another thread, which allows\\n165:  * the consumer to continue calling {@link #poll(Duration) poll} while the processor is still working.\\n166:  * Some care must be taken to ensure that committed offsets do not get ahead of the actual position.\\n167:  * Typically, you must disable automatic commits and manually commit processed offsets for records only after the\\n168:  * thread has finished handling them (depending on the delivery semantics you need).\\n169:  * Note also that you will need to {@link #pause(Collection) pause} the partition so that no new records are received\\n170:  * from poll until after thread has finished handling those previously returned.\\n171:  *\\n172:  * <h3>Usage Examples</h3>\\n173:  * The consumer APIs offer flexibility to cover a variety of consumption use cases. Here are some examples to\\n174:  * demonstrate how to use them.\\n175:  *\\n176:  * <h4>Automatic Offset Committing</h4>\\n177:  * This example demonstrates a simple usage of Kafka's consumer api that relies on automatic offset committing.\\n178:  * <p>\\n179:  * <pre>\\n180:  * {@code\\n181:  *     Properties props = new Properties();\\n182:  *     props.setProperty(\\\"bootstrap.servers\\\", \\\"localhost:9092\\\");\\n183:  *     props.setProperty(\\\"group.id\\\", \\\"test\\\");\\n184:  *     props.setProperty(\\\"enable.auto.commit\\\", \\\"true\\\");\\n185:  *     props.setProperty(\\\"auto.commit.interval.ms\\\", \\\"1000\\\");\\n186:  *     props.setProperty(\\\"key.deserializer\\\", \\\"org.apache.kafka.common.serialization.StringDeserializer\\\");\\n187:  *     props.setProperty(\\\"value.deserializer\\\", \\\"org.apache.kafka.common.serialization.StringDeserializer\\\");\\n188:  *     KafkaConsumer<String, String> consumer = new KafkaConsumer<>(props);\\n189:  *     consumer.subscribe(Arrays.asList(\\\"foo\\\", \\\"bar\\\"));\\n190:  *     while (true) {\\n191:  *         ConsumerRecords<String, String> records = consumer.poll(Duration.ofMillis(100));\\n192:  *         for (ConsumerRecord<String, String> record : records)\\n193:  *             System.out.printf(\\\"offset = %d, key = %s, value = %s%n\\\", record.offset(), record.key(), record.value());\\n194:  *     }\\n195:  * }</pre>\\n196:  *\\n197:  * The connection to the cluster is bootstrapped by specifying a list of one or more brokers to contact using the\\n198:  * configuration {@code bootstrap.servers}. This list is just used to discover the rest of the brokers in the\\n199:  * cluster and need not be an exhaustive list of servers in the cluster (though you may want to specify more than one in\\n200:  * case there are servers down when the client is connecting).\\n201: \",\"url\":\"https://sourcegraph.sourcegraph.com/github.com/sg-evals/kafka--e678b4b@47ac12bd6042edf23f1dbca3d789db945d62802d/-/blob/clients/src/main/java/org/apache/kafka/clients/consumer/KafkaConsumer.java%3FL1-200\"}"}]},"parent_tool_use_id":null,"session_id":"e1e96863-4dd1-457a-9aaf-69ff79f5231b","uuid":"b8603842-ce78-40f3-9dea-35937116b060","tool_use_result":{"content":"{\"content\":\"1: /*\\n2:  * Licensed to the Apache Software Foundation (ASF) under one or more\\n3:  * contributor license agreements.
