See <a href=\\\"#multithreaded\\\">Multi-threaded Processing</a> for more details.\\n63:  *\\n64:  * <h3>Cross-Version Compatibility</h3>\\n65:  * This client can communicate with brokers that are version 0.10.0 or newer. Older or newer brokers may not support\\n66:  * certain features. For example, 0.10.0 brokers do not support offsetsForTimes, because this feature was added\\n67:  * in version 0.10.1. You will receive an {@link org.apache.kafka.common.errors.UnsupportedVersionException}\\n68:  * when invoking an API that is not available on the running broker version.\\n69:  * <p>\\n70:  *\\n71:  * <h3>Offsets and Consumer Position</h3>\\n72:  * Kafka maintains a numerical offset for each record in a partition. This offset acts as a unique identifier of\\n73:  * a record within that partition, and also denotes the position of the consumer in the partition. For example, a consumer\\n74:  * which is at position 5 has consumed records with offsets 0 through 4 and will next receive the record with offset 5.\\n75:  * Note that offsets are not guaranteed to be consecutive (such as compacted topic or when records have been produced\\n76:  * using transactions). For example, if the consumer did read a record with offset 4, but 5 is not an offset\\n77:  * with a record, its position might advance to 6 (or higher) directly. Similarly, if the consumer's position is 5,\\n78:  * but there is no record with offset 5, the consumer will return the record with the next higher offset.\\n79:  * There are actually two notions of position relevant to the user of the consumer:\\n80:  * <p>\\n81:  * The {@link #position(TopicPartition) position} of the consumer gives the offset of the next record that will be given\\n82:  * out. It will be one larger than the highest offset the consumer has seen in that partition. It automatically advances\\n83:  * every time the consumer receives messages in a call to {@link #poll(Duration)}.\\n84:  * <p>\\n85:  * The {@link #commitSync() committed position} is the last offset that has been stored securely. Should the\\n86:  * process fail and restart, this is the offset that the consumer will recover to. The consumer can either automatically commit\\n87:  * offsets periodically; or it can choose to control this committed position manually by calling one of the commit APIs\\n88:  * (e.g. {@link #commitSync() commitSync} and {@link #commitAsync(OffsetCommitCallback) commitAsync}).\\n89:  * <p>\\n90:  * This distinction gives the consumer control over when a record is considered consumed. It is discussed in further\\n91:  * detail below.\\n92:  *\\n93:  * <h3><a name=\\\"consumergroups\\\">Consumer Groups and Topic Subscriptions</a></h3>\\n94:  *\\n95:  * Kafka uses the concept of <i>consumer groups</i> to allow a pool of processes to divide the work of consuming and\\n96:  * processing records. These processes can either be running on the same machine or they can be\\n97:  * distributed over many machines to provide scalability and fault tolerance for processing. All consumer instances\\n98:  * sharing the same {@code group.id} will be part of the same consumer group.\\n99:  * <p>\\n100:  * Each consumer in a group can dynamically set the list of topics it wants to subscribe to through one of the\\n101:  * {@link #subscribe(Collection, ConsumerRebalanceListener) subscribe} APIs. Kafka will deliver each message in the\\n102:  * subscribed topics to one process in each consumer group. This is achieved by balancing the partitions between all\\n103:  * members in the consumer group so that each partition is assigned to exactly one consumer in the group. So if there\\n104:  * is a topic with four partitions, and a consumer group with two processes, each process would consume from two partitions.\\n105:  * <p>\\n106:  * Membership in a consumer group is maintained dynamically: if a process fails, the partitions assigned to it will\\n107:  * be reassigned to other consumers in the same group. Similarly, if a new consumer joins the group, partitions will be moved\\n108:  * from existing consumers to the new one. This is known as <i>rebalancing</i> the group and is discussed in more\\n109:  * detail <a href=\\\"#failuredetection\\\">below</a>. Group rebalancing is also used when new partitions are added\\n110:  * to one of the subscribed topics or when a new topic matching a {@link #subscribe(Pattern, ConsumerRebalanceListener) subscribed regex}\\n111:  * is created. The group will automatically detect the new partitions through periodic metadata refreshes and\\n112:  * assign them to members of the group.\\n113:  * <p>\\n114:  * Conceptually you can think of a consumer group as being a single logical subscriber that happens to be made up of\\n115:  * multiple processes. As a multi-subscriber system, Kafka naturally supports having any number of consumer groups for a\\n116:  * given topic without duplicating data (additional consumers are actually quite cheap).\\n117:  * <p>\\n118:  * This is a slight generalization of the functionality that is common in messaging systems. To get semantics similar to\\n119:  * a queue in a traditional messaging system all processes would be part of a single consumer group and hence record\\n120:  * delivery would be balanced over the group like with a queue. Unlike a traditional messaging system, though, you can\\n121:  * have multiple such groups. To get semantics similar to pub-sub in a traditional messaging system each process would\\n122:  * have its own consumer group, so each process would subscribe to all the records published to the topic.\\n123:  * <p>\\n124:  * In addition, when group reassignment happens automatically, consumers can be notified through a {@link ConsumerRebalanceListener},\\n125:  * which allows them to finish necessary application-level logic such as state cleanup, manual offset\\n126:  * commits, etc. See <a href=\\\"#rebalancecallback\\\">Storing Offsets Outside Kafka</a> for more details.\\n127:  * <p>\\n128:  * It is also possible for the consumer to <a href=\\\"#manualassignment\\\">manually assign</a> specific partitions\\n129:  * (similar to the older \\\"simple\\\" consumer) using {@link #assign(Collection)}. In this case, dynamic partition\\n130:  * assignment and consumer group coordination will be disabled.\\n131:  *\\n132:  * <h3><a name=\\\"failuredetection\\\">Detecting Consumer Failures</a></h3>\\n133:  *\\n134:  * After subscribing to a set of topics, the consumer will automatically join the group when {@link #poll(Duration)} is\\n135:  * invoked. The poll API is designed to ensure consumer liveness. As long as you continue to call poll, the consumer\\n136:  * will stay in the group and continue to receive messages from the partitions it was assigned. Underneath the covers,\\n137:  * the consumer sends periodic heartbeats to the server. If the consumer crashes or is unable to send heartbeats for\\n138:  * a duration of {@code session.timeout.ms}, then the consumer will be considered dead and its partitions will\\n139:  * be reassigned.\\n140:  * <p>\\n141:  * It is also possible that the consumer could encounter a \\\"livelock\\\" situation where it is continuing\\n142:  * to send heartbeats, but no progress is being made. To prevent the consumer from holding onto its partitions\\n143:  * indefinitely in this case, we provide a liveness detection mechanism using the {@code max.poll.interval.ms}\\n144:  * setting. Basically if you don't call poll at least as frequently as the configured max interval,\\n145:  * then the client will proactively leave the group so that another consumer can take over its partitions. When this happens,\\n146:  * you may see an offset commit failure (as indicated by a {@link CommitFailedException} thrown from a call to {@link #commitSync()}).\\n147:  * This is a safety mechanism which guarantees that only active members of the group are able to commit offsets.\\n148:  * So to stay in the group, you must continue to call poll.\\n149:  * <p>\\n150:  * The consumer provides two configuration settings to control the behavior of the poll loop:\\n151:  * <ol>\\n152:  *     <li><code>max.poll.interval.ms</code>: By increasing the interval between expected polls, you can give\\n153:  *     the consumer more time to handle a batch of records returned from {@link #poll(Duration)}. The drawback\\n154:  *     is that increasing this value may delay a group rebalance since the consumer will only join the rebalance\\n155:  *     inside the call to poll. You can use this setting to bound the time to finish a rebalance, but\\n156:  *     you risk slower progress if the consumer cannot actually call {@link #poll(Duration) poll} often enough.</li>\\n157:  *     <li><code>max.poll.records</code>: Use this setting to limit the total records returned from a single\\n158:  *     call to poll. This can make it easier to predict the maximum that must be handled within each poll\\n159:  *     interval. By tuning this value, you may be able to reduce the poll interval, which will reduce the\\n160:  *     impact of group rebalancing.</li>\\n161:  * </ol>\\n162:  * <p>\\n163:  * For use cases where message processing time varies unpredictably, neither of these options may be sufficient.\\n164:  * The recommended way to handle these cases is to move message processing to another thread, which allows\\n165:  * the consumer to continue calling {@link #poll(Duration) poll} while the processor is still working.\\n166:  * Some care must be taken to ensure that committed offsets do not get ahead of the actual position.\\n167:  * Typically, you must disable automatic commits and manually commit processed offsets for records only after the\\n168:  * thread has finished handling them (depending on the delivery semantics you need).\\n169:  * Note also that you will need to {@link #pause(Collection) pause} the partition so that no new records are received\\n170:  * from poll until after thread has finished handling those previously returned.\\n171:  *\\n172:  * <h3>Usage Examples</h3>\\n173:  * The consumer APIs offer flexibility to cover a variety of consumption use cases. Here are some examples to\\n174:  * demonstrate how to use them.\\n175:  *\\n176:  * <h4>Automatic Offset Committing</h4>\\n177:  * This example demonstrates a simple usage of Kafka's consumer api that relies on automatic offset committing.\\n178:  * <p>\\n179:  * <pre>\\n180:  * {@code\\n181:  *     Properties props = new Properties();\\n182:  *     props.setProperty(\\\"bootstrap.servers\\\", \\\"localhost:9092\\\");\\n183:  *     props.setProperty(\\\"group.id\\\", \\\"test\\\");\\n184:  *     props.setProperty(\\\"enable.auto.commit\\\", \\\"true\\\");\\n185:  *     props.setProperty(\\\"auto.commit.interval.ms\\\", \\\"1000\\\");\\n186:  *     props.setProperty(\\\"key.deserializer\\\", \\\"org.apache.kafka.common.serialization.StringDeserializer\\\");\\n187:  *     props.setProperty(\\\"value.deserializer\\\", \\\"org.apache.kafka.common.serialization.StringDeserializer\\\");\\n188:  *     KafkaConsumer<String, String> consumer = new KafkaConsumer<>(props);\\n189:  *     consumer.subscribe(Arrays.asList(\\\"foo\\\", \\\"bar\\\"));\\n190:  *     while (true) {\\n191:  *         ConsumerRecords<String, String> records = consumer.poll(Duration.ofMillis(100));\\n192:  *         for (ConsumerRecord<String, String> record : records)\\n193:  *             System.out.printf(\\\"offset = %d, key = %s, value = %s%n\\\", record.offset(), record.key(), record.value());\\n194:  *     }\\n195:  * }</pre>\\n196:  *\\n197:  * The connection to the cluster is bootstrapped by specifying a list of one or more brokers to contact using the\\n198:  * configuration {@code bootstrap.servers}. This list is just used to discover the rest of the brokers in the\\n199:  * cluster and need not be an exhaustive list of servers in the cluster (though you may want to specify more than one in\\n200:  * case there are servers down when the client is connecting).\\n201: \",\"url\":\"https://sourcegraph.sourcegraph.com/github.com/sg-evals/kafka--e678b4b@47ac12bd6042edf23f1dbca3d789db945d62802d/-/blob/clients/src/main/java/org/apache/kafka/clients/consumer/KafkaConsumer.java%3FL1-200\"}","structuredContent":{"content":"1: /*\n2:  * Licensed to the Apache Software Foundation (ASF) under one or more\n3:  * contributor license agreements. If we allowed offsets to auto commit as in the previous example, records\\n245:  * would be considered consumed after they were returned to the user in {@link #poll(Duration) poll}. It would then be\\n246:  * possible\\n247:  * for our process to fail after batching the records, but before they had been inserted into the database.\\n248:  * <p>\\n249:  * To avoid this, we will manually commit the offsets only after the corresponding records have been inserted into the\\n250:  * database. This gives us exact control of when a record is considered consumed. This raises the opposite possibility:\\n251:  * the process could fail in the interval after the insert into the database but before the commit (even though this\\n252:  * would likely just be a few milliseconds, it is a possibility). In this case the process that took over consumption\\n253:  * would consume from last committed offset and would repeat the insert of the last batch of data. Used in this way\\n254:  * Kafka provides what is often called \\\"at-least-once\\\" delivery guarantees, as each record will likely be delivered one\\n255:  * time but in failure cases could be duplicated.\\n256:  * <p>\\n257:  * <b>Note: Using automatic offset commits can also give you \\\"at-least-once\\\" delivery, but the requirement is that\\n258:  * you must consume all data returned from each call to {@link #poll(Duration)} before any subsequent calls, or before\\n259:  * {@link #close() closing} the consumer. If you fail to do either of these, it is possible for the committed offset\\n260:  * to get ahead of the consumed position, which results in missing records. The advantage of using manual offset\\n261:  * control is that you have direct control over when a record is considered \\\"consumed.\\\"</b>\\n262:  * <p>\\n263:  * The above example uses {@link #commitSync() commitSync} to mark all received records as committed. Partition Assignment</a></h4>\\n292:  *\\n293:  * In the previous examples, we subscribed to the topics we were interested in and let Kafka dynamically assign a\\n294:  * fair share of the partitions for those topics based on the active consumers in the group. However, in\\n295:  * some cases you may need finer control over the specific partitions that are assigned. For example:\\n296:  * <p>\\n297:  * <ul>\\n298:  * <li>If the process is maintaining some kind of local state associated with that partition (like a\\n299:  * local on-disk key-value store), then it should only get records for the partition it is maintaining on disk.\\n300:  * <li>If the process itself is highly available and will be restarted if it fails (perhaps using a\\n301:  * cluster management framework like YARN, Mesos, or AWS facilities, or as part of a stream processing framework). In\\n302:  * this case there is no need for Kafka to detect the failure and reassign the partition since the consuming process\\n303:  * will be restarted on another machine.\\n304:  * </ul>\\n305:  * <p>\\n306:  * To use this mode, instead of subscribing to the topic using {@link #subscribe(Collection) subscribe}, you just call\\n307:  * {@link #assign(Collection)} with the full list of partitions that you want to consume.\\n308:  *\\n309:  * <pre>\\n310:  * {@code\\n311:  *     String topic = \\\"foo\\\";\\n312:  *     TopicPartition partition0 = new TopicPartition(topic, 0);\\n313:  *     TopicPartition partition1 = new TopicPartition(topic, 1);\\n314:  *     consumer.assign(Arrays.asList(partition0, partition1));\\n315:  * }</pre>\\n316:  *\\n317:  * Once assigned, you can call {@link #poll(Duration) poll} in a loop, just as in the preceding examples to consume\\n318:  * records. The group that the consumer specifies is still used for committing offsets, but now the set of partitions\\n319:  * will only change with another call to {@link #assign(Collection) assign}. Manual partition assignment does\\n320:  * not use group coordination, so consumer failures will not cause assigned partitions to be rebalanced. Each consumer\\n321:  * acts independently even if it shares a groupId with another consumer. To avoid offset commit conflicts, you should\\n322:  * usually ensure that the groupId is unique for each consumer instance.\\n323:  * <p>\\n324:  * Note that it isn't possible to mix manual partition assignment (i.e. using {@link #assign(Collection) assign})\\n325:  * with dynamic partition assignment through topic subscription (i.e. using {@link #subscribe(Collection) subscribe}).\\n326:  *\\n327:  * <h4><a name=\\\"rebalancecallback\\\">Storing Offsets Outside Kafka</h4>\\n328:  *\\n329:  * The consumer application need not use Kafka's built-in offset storage, it can store offsets in a store of its own\\n330:  * choosing. The primary use case for this is allowing the application to store both the offset and the results of the\\n331:  * consumption in the same system in a way that both the results and offsets are stored atomically. This is not always\\n332:  * possible, but when it is it will make the consumption fully atomic and give \\\"exactly once\\\" semantics that are\\n333:  * stronger than the default \\\"at-least once\\\" semantics you get with Kafka's offset commit functionality.\\n334:  * <p>\\n335:  * Here are a couple of examples of this type of usage:\\n336:  * <ul>\\n337:  * <li>If the results of the consumption are being stored in a relational database, storing the offset in the database\\n338:  * as well can allow committing both the results and offset in a single transaction. Thus either the transaction will\\n339:  * succeed and the offset will be updated based on what was consumed or the result will not be stored and the offset\\n340:  * won't be updated.\\n341:  * <li>If the results are being stored in a local store it may be possible to store the offset there as well. For\\n342:  * example a search index could be built by subscribing to a particular partition and storing both the offset and the\\n343:  * indexed data together. If this is done in a way that is atomic, it is often possible to have it be the case that even\\n344:  * if a crash occurs that causes unsync'd data to be lost, whatever is left has the corresponding offset stored as well.\\n345:  * This means that in this case the indexing process that comes back having lost recent updates just resumes indexing\\n346:  * from what it has ensuring that no updates are lost.\\n347:  * </ul>\\n348:  * <p>\\n349:  * Each record comes with its own offset, so to manage your own offset you just need to do the following:\\n350:  *\\n351:  * <ul>\\n352:  * <li>Configure <code>enable.auto.commit=false</code>\\n353:  * <li>Use the offset provided with each {@link ConsumerRecord} to save your position.\\n354:  * <li>On restart restore the position of the consumer using {@link #seek(TopicPartition, long)}.\\n355:  * </ul>\\n356:  *\\n357:  * <p>\\n358:  * This type of usage is simplest when the partition assignment is also done manually (this would be likely in the\\n359:  * search index use case described above). If the partition assignment is done automatically special care is\\n360:  * needed to handle the case where partition assignments change. This can be done by providing a\\n361:  * {@link ConsumerRebalanceListener} instance in the call to {@link #subscribe(Collection, ConsumerRebalanceListener)}\\n362:  * and {@link #subscribe(Pattern, ConsumerRebalanceListener)}.\\n363:  * For example, when partitions are taken from a consumer the consumer will want to commit its offset for those partitions by\\n364:  * implementing {@link ConsumerRebalanceListener#onPartitionsRevoked(Collection)}. When partitions are assigned to a\\n365:  * consumer, the consumer will want to look up the offset for those new partitions and correctly initialize the consumer\\n366:  * to that position by implementing {@link ConsumerRebalanceListener#onPartitionsAssigned(Collection)}.\\n367:  * <p>\\n368:  * Another common use for {@link ConsumerRebalanceListener} is to flush any caches the application maintains for\\n369:  * partitions that are moved elsewhere.\\n370:  *\\n371:  * <h4>Controlling The Consumer's Position</h4>\\n372:  *\\n373:  * In most use cases the consumer will simply consume records from beginning to end, periodically committing its\\n374:  * position (either automatically or manually). However Kafka allows the consumer to manually control its position,\\n375:  * moving forward or backwards in a partition at will. This means a consumer can re-consume older records, or skip to\\n376:  * the most recent records without actually consuming the intermediate records.\\n377:  * <p>\\n378:  * There are several instances where manually controlling the consumer's position can be useful.\\n379:  * <p>\\n380:  * One case is for time-sensitive record processing it may make sense for a consumer that falls far enough behind to not\\n381:  * attempt to catch up processing all records, but rather just skip to the most recent records.\\n382:  * <p>\\n383:  * Another use case is for a system that maintains local state as described in the previous section. In such a system\\n384:  * the consumer will want to initialize its position on start-up to whatever is contained in the local store. Likewise\\n385:  * if the local state is destroyed (say because the disk is lost) the state may be recreated on a new machine by\\n386:  * re-consuming all the data and recreating the state (assuming that Kafka is retaining sufficient history).\\n387:  * <p>\\n388:  * Kafka allows specifying the position using {@link #seek(TopicPartition, long)} to specify the new position. Special\\n389:  * methods for seeking to the earliest and latest offset the server maintains are also available (\\n390:  * {@link #seekToBeginning(Collection)} and {@link #seekToEnd(Collection)} respectively).\\n391:  *\\n392:  * <h4>Consumption Flow Control</h4>\\n393:  *\\n394:  * If a consumer is assigned multiple partitions to fetch data from, it will try to consume from all of them at the same time,\\n395:  * effectively giving these partitions the same priority for consumption. However in some cases consumers may want to\\n396:  * first focus on fetching from some subset of the assigned partitions at full speed, and only start fetching other partitions\\n397:  * when these partitions have few or no data to consume.\\n398:  *\\n399:  * <p>\\n400:  * One of such cases is stream processing, where processor fetches from two topics and performs the join on these two streams.\\n401: \",\"url\":\"https://sourcegraph.sourcegraph.com/github.com/sg-evals/kafka--e678b4b@47ac12bd6042edf23f1dbca3d789db945d62802d/-/blob/clients/src/main/java/org/apache/kafka/clients/consumer/KafkaConsumer.java%3FL200-400\"}"}]},"parent_tool_use_id":null,"session_id":"e1e96863-4dd1-457a-9aaf-69ff79f5231b","uuid":"ae029b6d-c684-426a-bc97-8a0eaab16620","tool_use_result":{"content":"{\"content\":\"200:  * case there are servers down when the client is connecting).\\n201:  * <p>\\n202:  * Setting {@code enable.auto.commit} means that offsets are committed automatically with a frequency controlled by\\n203:  * the config {@code auto.commit.interval.ms}.\\n204:  * <p>\\n205:  * In this example the consumer is subscribing to the topics <i>foo</i> and <i>bar</i> as part of a group of consumers\\n206:  * called <i>test</i> as configured with {@code group.id}.\\n207:  * <p>\\n208:  * The deserializer settings specify how to turn bytes into objects. Thus either the transaction will\\n339:  * succeed and the offset will be updated based on what was consumed or the result will not be stored and the offset\\n340:  * won't be updated.\\n341:  * <li>If the results are being stored in a local store it may be possible to store the offset there as well. For\\n342:  * example a search index could be built by subscribing to a particular partition and storing both the offset and the\\n343:  * indexed data together. If this is done in a way that is atomic, it is often possible to have it be the case that even\\n344:  * if a crash occurs that causes unsync'd data to be lost, whatever is left has the corresponding offset stored as well.\\n345:  * This means that in this case the indexing process that comes back having lost recent updates just resumes indexing\\n346:  * from what it has ensuring that no updates are lost.\\n347:  * </ul>\\n348:  * <p>\\n349:  * Each record comes with its own offset, so to manage your own offset you just need to do the following:\\n350:  *\\n351:  * <ul>\\n352:  * <li>Configure <code>enable.auto.commit=false</code>\\n353:  * <li>Use the offset provided with each {@link ConsumerRecord} to save your position.\\n354:  * <li>On restart restore the position of the consumer using {@link #seek(TopicPartition, long)}.\\n355:  * </ul>\\n356:  *\\n357:  * <p>\\n358:  * This type of usage is simplest when the partition assignment is also done manually (this would be likely in the\\n359:  * search index use case described above). If the partition assignment is done automatically special care is\\n360:  * needed to handle the case where partition assignments change. This can be done by providing a\\n361:  * {@link ConsumerRebalanceListener} instance in the call to {@link #subscribe(Collection, ConsumerRebalanceListener)}\\n362:  * and {@link #subscribe(Pattern, ConsumerRebalanceListener)}.\\n363:  * For example, when partitions are taken from a consumer the consumer will want to commit its offset for those partitions by\\n364:  * implementing {@link ConsumerRebalanceListener#onPartitionsRevoked(Collection)}. When partitions are assigned to a\\n365:  * consumer, the consumer will want to look up the offset for those new partitions and correctly initialize the consumer\\n366:  * to that position by implementing {@link ConsumerRebalanceListener#onPartitionsAssigned(Collection)}.\\n367:  * <p>\\n368:  * Another common use for {@link ConsumerRebalanceListener} is to flush any caches the application maintains for\\n369:  * partitions that are moved elsewhere.\\n370:  *\\n371:  * <h4>Controlling The Consumer's Position</h4>\\n372:  *\\n373:  * In most use cases the consumer will simply consume records from beginning to end, periodically committing its\\n374:  * position (either automatically or manually). However Kafka allows the consumer to manually control its position,\\n375:  * moving forward or backwards in a partition at will. This means a consumer can re-consume older records, or skip to\\n376:  * the most recent records without actually consuming the intermediate records.\\n377:  * <p>\\n378:  * There are several instances where manually controlling the consumer's position can be useful.\\n379:  * <p>\\n380:  * One case is for time-sensitive record processing it may make sense for a consumer that falls far enough behind to not\\n381:  * attempt to catch up processing all records, but rather just skip to the most recent records.\\n382:  * <p>\\n383:  * Another use case is for a system that maintains local state as described in the previous section. In such a system\\n384:  * the consumer will want to initialize its position on start-up to whatever is contained in the local store. Likewise\\n385:  * if the local state is destroyed (say because the disk is lost) the state may be recreated on a new machine by\\n386:  * re-consuming all the data and recreating the state (assuming that Kafka is retaining sufficient history).\\n387:  * <p>\\n388:  * Kafka allows specifying the position using {@link #seek(TopicPartition, long)} to specify the new position. Special\\n389:  * methods for seeking to the earliest and latest offset the server maintains are also available (\\n390:  * {@link #seekToBeginning(Collection)} and {@link #seekToEnd(Collection)} respectively).\\n391:  *\\n392:  * <h4>Consumption Flow Control</h4>\\n393:  *\\n394:  * If a consumer is assigned multiple partitions to fetch data from, it will try to consume from all of them at the same time,\\n395:  * effectively giving these partitions the same priority for consumption. However in some cases consumers may want to\\n396:  * first focus on fetching from some subset of the assigned partitions at full speed, and only start fetching other partitions\\n397:  * when these partitions have few or no data to consume.\\n398:  *\\n399:  * <p>\\n400:  * One of such cases is stream processing, where processor fetches from two topics and performs the join on these two streams.\\n401: \",\"url\":\"https://sourcegraph.sourcegraph.com/github.com/sg-evals/kafka--e678b4b@47ac12bd6042edf23f1dbca3d789db945d62802d/-/blob/clients/src/main/java/org/apache/kafka/clients/consumer/KafkaConsumer.java%3FL200-400\"}","structuredContent":{"content":"200:  * case there are servers down when the client is connecting).\n201:  * <p>\n202:  * Setting {@code enable.auto.commit} means that offsets are committed automatically with a frequency controlled by\n203:  * the config {@code auto.commit.interval.ms}.\n204:  * <p>\n205:  * In this example the consumer is subscribing to the topics <i>foo</i> and <i>bar</i> as part of a group of consumers\n206:  * called <i>test</i> as configured with {@code group.id}.\n207:  * <p>\n208:  * The deserializer settings specify how to turn bytes into objects. For example, by specifying string deserializers, we\n209:  * are saying that our record's key and value will just be simple strings.\n210:  *\n211:  * <h4>Manual Offset Control</h4>\n212:  *\n213:  * Instead of relying on the consumer to periodically commit consumed offsets, users can also control when records\n214:  * should be considered as consumed and hence commit their offsets. This is useful when the consumption of the messages\n215:  * is coupled with some processing logic and hence a message should not be considered as consumed until it is completed processing.\n216: \n217:  * <p>\n218:  * <pre>\n219:  * {@code\n220:  *     Properties props = new Properties();\n221:  *     props.setProperty(\"bootstrap.servers\", \"localhost:9092\");\n222:  *     props.setProperty(\"group.id\", \"test\");\n223:  *     props.setProperty(\"enable.auto.commit\", \"false\");\n224:  *     props.setProperty(\"key.deserializer\", \"org.apache.kafka.common.serialization.StringDeserializer\");\n225:  *     props.setProperty(\"value.deserializer\", \"org.apache.kafka.common.serialization.StringDeserializer\");\n226:  *     KafkaConsumer<String, String> consumer = new KafkaConsumer<>(props);\n227:  *     consumer.subscribe(Arrays.asList(\"foo\", \"bar\"));\n228:  *     final int minBatchSize = 200;\n229:  *     List<ConsumerRecord<String, String>> buffer = new ArrayList<>();\n230:  *     while (true) {\n231:  *         ConsumerRecords<String, String> records = consumer.poll(Duration.ofMillis(100));\n232:  *         for (ConsumerRecord<String, String> record : records) {\n233:  *             buffer.add(record);\n234:  *         }\n235:  *         if (buffer.size() >= minBatchSize) {\n236:  *             insertIntoDb(buffer);\n237:  *             consumer.commitSync();\n238:  *             buffer.clear();\n239:  *         }\n240:  *     }\n241:  * }</pre>\n242:  *\n243:  * In this example we will consume a batch of records and batch them up in memory. When we have enough records\n244:  * batched, we will insert them into a database. If we allowed offsets to auto commit as in the previous example, records\n245:  * would be considered consumed after they were returned to the user in {@link #poll(Duration) poll}. It would then be\n246:  * possible\n247:  * for our process to fail after batching the records, but before they had been inserted into the database.\n248:  * <p>\n249:  * To avoid this, we will manually commit the offsets only after the corresponding records have been inserted into the\n250:  * database. This gives us exact control of when a record is considered consumed. This raises the opposite possibility:\n251:  * the process could fail in the interval after the insert into the database but before the commit (even though this\n252:  * would likely just be a few milliseconds, it is a possibility). In this case the process that took over consumption\n253:  * would consume from last committed offset and would repeat the insert of the last batch of data. Used in this way\n254:  * Kafka provides what is often called \"at-least-once\" delivery guarantees, as each record will likely be delivered one\n255:  * time but in failure cases could be duplicated.\n256:  * <p>\n257:  * <b>Note: Using automatic offset commits can also give you \"at-least-once\" delivery, but the requirement is that\n258:  * you must consume all data returned from each call to {@link #poll(Duration)} before any subsequent calls, or before\n259:  * {@link #close() closing} the consumer. If you fail to do either of these, it is possible for the committed offset\n260:  * to get ahead of the consumed position, which results in missing records. The advantage of using manual offset\n261:  * control is that you have direct control over when a record is considered \"consumed.\"</b>\n262:  * <p>\n263:  * The above example uses {@link #commitSync() commitSync} to mark all received records as committed. In some cases\n264:  * you may wish to have even finer control over which records have been committed by specifying an offset explicitly.\n265:  * In the example below we commit offset after we finish handling the records in each partition.\n266:  * <p>\n267:  * <pre>\n268:  * {@code\n269:  *     try {\n270:  *         while(running) {\n271:  *             ConsumerRecords<String, String> records = consumer.poll(Duration.ofMillis(Long.MAX_VALUE));\n272:  *             for (TopicPartition partition : records.partitions()) {\n273:  *                 List<ConsumerRecord<String, String>> partitionRecords = records.records(partition);\n274:  *                 for (ConsumerRecord<String, String> record : partitionRecords) {\n275:  *                     System.out.println(record.offset() + \": \" + record.value());\n276:  *                 }\n277:  *                 consumer.commitSync(Collections.singletonMap(partition, records.nextOffsets().get(partition)));\n278:  *             }\n279:  *         }\n280:  *     } finally {\n281:  *       consumer.close();\n282:  *     }\n283:  * }</pre>\n284:  *\n285:  * <b>Note: The committed offset should always be the offset of the next message that your application will read.</b>\n286:  * Thus, when calling {@link #commitSync(Map) commitSync(offsets)} you should use {@code nextRecordToBeProcessed.offset()}\n287:  * or if {@link ConsumerRecords} is exhausted already {@link ConsumerRecords#nextOffsets()} instead.\n288:  * You should also add the leader epoch as commit metadata, which can be obtained from\n289:  * {@link ConsumerRecord#leaderEpoch()} or {@link ConsumerRecords#nextOffsets()}.\n290:  *\n291:  * <h4><a name=\"manualassignment\">Manual Partition Assignment</a></h4>\n292:  *\n293:  * In the previous examples, we subscribed to the topics we were interested in and let Kafka dynamically assign a\n294:  * fair share of the partitions for those topics based on the active consumers in the group. However, in\n295:  * some cases you may need finer control over the specific partitions that are assigned. For example:\n296:  * <p>\n297:  * <ul>\n298:  * <li>If the process is maintaining some kind of local state associated with that partition (like a\n299:  * local on-disk key-value store), then it should only get records for the partition it is maintaining on disk.\n300:  * <li>If the process itself is highly available and will be restarted if it fails (perhaps using a\n301:  * cluster management framework like YARN, Mesos, or AWS facilities, or as part of a stream processing framework). In\n302:  * this case there is no need for Kafka to detect the failure and reassign the partition since the consuming process\n303:  * will be restarted on another machine.\n304:  * </ul>\n305:  * <p>\n306:  * To use this mode, instead of subscribing to the topic using {@link #subscribe(Collection) subscribe}, you just call\n307:  * {@link #assign(Collection)} with the full list of partitions that you want to consume.\n308:  *\n309:  * <pre>\n310:  * {@code\n311:  *     String topic = \"foo\";\n312:  *     TopicPartition partition0 = new TopicPartition(topic, 0);\n313:  *     TopicPartition partition1 = new TopicPartition(topic, 1);\n314:  *     consumer.assign(Arrays.asList(partition0, partition1));\n315:  * }</pre>\n316:  *\n317:  * Once assigned, you can call {@link #poll(Duration) poll} in a loop, just as in the preceding examples to consume\n318:  * records. The group that the consumer specifies is still used for committing offsets, but now the set of partitions\n319:  * will only change with another call to {@link #assign(Collection) assign}. Manual partition assignment does\n320:  * not use group coordination, so consumer failures will not cause assigned partitions to be rebalanced. Each consumer\n321:  * acts independently even if it shares a groupId with another consumer. To avoid offset commit conflicts, you should\n322:  * usually ensure that the groupId is unique for each consumer instance.\n323:  * <p>\n324:  * Note that it isn't possible to mix manual partition assignment (i.e. using {@link #assign(Collection) assign})\n325:  * with dynamic partition assignment through topic subscription (i.e. using {@link #subscribe(Collection) subscribe}).\n326:  *\n327:  * <h4><a name=\"rebalancecallback\">Storing Offsets Outside Kafka</h4>\n328:  *\n329:  * The consumer application need not use Kafka's built-in offset storage, it can store offsets in a store of its own\n330:  * choosing. The primary use case for this is allowing the application to store both the offset and the results of the\n331:  * consumption in the same system in a way that both the results and offsets are stored atomically. This is not always\n332:  * possible, but when it is it will make the consumption fully atomic and give \"exactly once\" semantics that are\n333:  * stronger than the default \"at-least once\" semantics you get with Kafka's offset commit functionality.\n334:  * <p>\n335:  * Here are a couple of examples of this type of usage:\n336:  * <ul>\n337:  * <li>If the results of the consumption are being stored in a relational database, storing the offset in the database\n338:  * as well can allow committing both the results and offset in a single transaction. Thus either the transaction will\n339:  * succeed and the offset will be updated based on what was consumed or the result will not be stored and the offset\n340:  * won't be updated.\n341:  * <li>If the results are being stored in a local store it may be possible to store the offset there as well. For\n342:  * example a search index could be built by subscribing to a particular partition and storing both the offset and the\n343:  * indexed data together. If this is done in a way that is atomic, it is often possible to have it be the case that even\n344:  * if a crash occurs that causes unsync'd data to be lost, whatever is left has the corresponding offset stored as well.\n345:  * This means that in this case the indexing process that comes back having lost recent updates just resumes indexing\n346:  * from what it has ensuring that no updates are lost.\n347:  * </ul>\n348:  * <p>\n349:  * Each record comes with its own offset, so to manage your own offset you just need to do the following:\n350:  *\n351:  * <ul>\n352:  * <li>Configure <code>enable.auto.commit=false</code>\n353:  * <li>Use the offset provided with each {@link ConsumerRecord} to save your position.\n354:  * <li>On restart restore the position of the consumer using {@link #seek(TopicPartition, long)}.\n355:  * </ul>\n356:  *\n357:  * <p>\n358:  * This type of usage is simplest when the partition assignment is also done manually (this would be likely in the\n359:  * search index use case described above). If the partition assignment is done automatically special care is\n360:  * needed to handle the case where partition assignments change. This can be done by providing a\n361:  * {@link ConsumerRebalanceListener} instance in the call to {@link #subscribe(Collection, ConsumerRebalanceListener)}\n362:  * and {@link #subscribe(Pattern, ConsumerRebalanceListener)}.\n363:  * For example, when partitions are taken from a consumer the consumer will want to commit its offset for those partitions by\n364:  * implementing {@link ConsumerRebalanceListener#onPartitionsRevoked(Collection)}. When partitions are assigned to a\n365:  * consumer, the consumer will want to look up the offset for those new partitions and correctly initialize the consumer\n366:  * to that position by implementing {@link ConsumerRebalanceListener#onPartitionsAssigned(Collection)}.\n367:  * <p>\n368:  * Another common use for {@link ConsumerRebalanceListener} is to flush any caches the application maintains for\n369:  * partitions that are moved elsewhere.\n370:  *\n371:  * <h4>Controlling The Consumer's Position</h4>\n372:  *\n373:  * In most use cases the consumer will simply consume records from beginning to end, periodically committing its\n374:  * position (either automatically or manually). However Kafka allows the consumer to manually control its position,\n375:  * moving forward or backwards in a partition at will. This means a consumer can re-consume older records, or skip to\n376:  * the most recent records without actually consuming the intermediate records.\n377:  * <p>\n378:  * There are several instances where manually controlling the consumer's position can be useful.\n379:  * <p>\n380:  * One case is for time-sensitive record processing it may make sense for a consumer that falls far enough behind to not\n381:  * attempt to catch up processing all records, but rather just skip to the most recent records.\n382:  * <p>\n383:  * Another use case is for a system that maintains local state as described in the previous section. Special\n389:  * methods for seeking to the earliest and latest offset the server maintains are also available (\n390:  * {@link #seekToBeginning(Collection)} and {@link #seekToEnd(Collection)} respectively).\n391:  *\n392:  * <h4>Consumption Flow Control</h4>\n393:  *\n394:  * If a consumer is assigned multiple partitions to fetch data from, it will try to consume from all of them at the same time,\n395:  * effectively giving these partitions the same priority for consumption. {"type":"user","message":{"role":"user","content":[{"tool_use_id":"toolu_01BNpNmLwQKX3c88xt98Vvhd","type":"tool_result","content":"{\"content\":\"400:  * One of such cases is stream processing, where processor fetches from two topics and performs the join on these two streams.\\n401:  * When one of the topics is long lagging behind the other, the processor would like to pause fetching from the ahead topic\\n402:  * in order to get the lagging stream to catch up. Another example is bootstrapping upon consumer starting up where there are\\n403:  * a lot of history data to catch up, the applications usually want to get the latest data on some of the topics before consider\\n404:  * fetching other topics.\\n405:  *\\n406:  * <p>\\n407:  * Kafka supports dynamic controlling of consumption flows by using {@link #pause(Collection)} and {@link #resume(Collection)}\\n408:  * to pause the consumption on the specified assigned partitions and resume the consumption\\n409:  * on the specified paused partitions respectively in the future {@link #poll(Duration)} calls.\\n410:  *\\n411:  * <h3>Reading Transactional Messages</h3>\\n412:  *\\n413:  * <p>\\n414:  * Transactions were introduced in Kafka 0.11.0 wherein applications can write to multiple topics and partitions atomically.\\n415:  * In order for this to work, consumers reading from these partitions should be configured to only read committed data.\\n416:  * This can be achieved by setting the {@code isolation.level=read_committed} in the consumer's configuration.\\n417:  *\\n418:  * <p>\\n419:  * In <code>read_committed</code> mode, the consumer will read only those transactional messages which have been\\n420:  * successfully committed. It will continue to read non-transactional messages as before. There is no client-side\\n421:  * buffering in <code>read_committed</code> mode. Instead, the end offset of a partition for a <code>read_committed</code>\\n422:  * consumer would be the offset of the first message in the partition belonging to an open transaction. This offset\\n423:  * is known as the 'Last Stable Offset'(LSO).</p>\\n424:  *\\n425:  * <p>\\n426:  * A {@code read_committed} consumer will only read up to the LSO and filter out any transactional\\n427:  * messages which have been aborted. The LSO also affects the behavior of {@link #seekToEnd(Collection)} and\\n428:  * {@link #endOffsets(Collection)} for {@code read_committed} consumers, details of which are in each method's documentation.\\n429:  * Finally, the fetch lag metrics are also adjusted to be relative to the LSO for {@code read_committed} consumers.\\n430:  *\\n431:  * <p>\\n432:  * Partitions with transactional messages will include commit or abort markers which indicate the result of a transaction.\\n433:  * There markers are not returned to applications, yet have an offset in the log. As a result, applications reading from\\n434:  * topics with transactional messages will see gaps in the consumed offsets. These missing messages would be the transaction\\n435:  * markers, and they are filtered out for consumers in both isolation levels. Additionally, applications using\\n436:  * {@code read_committed} consumers may also see gaps due to aborted transactions, since those messages would not\\n437:  * be returned by the consumer and yet would have valid offsets.\\n438:  *\\n439:  * <h3><a name=\\\"multithreaded\\\">Multi-threaded Processing</a></h3>\\n440:  *\\n441:  * The Kafka consumer is NOT thread-safe. It is the responsibility of the user to ensure that multi-threaded access\\n442:  * is properly synchronized. Un-synchronized access will result in {@link ConcurrentModificationException}.\\n443:  *\\n444:  * <p>\\n445:  * The only exception to this rule is {@link #wakeup()}, which can safely be used from an external thread to\\n446:  * interrupt an active operation. In this case, a {@link org.apache.kafka.common.errors.WakeupException} will be\\n447:  * thrown from the thread blocking on the operation. This can be used to shutdown the consumer from another thread.\\n448:  * The following snippet shows the typical pattern:\\n449:  *\\n450:  * <pre>\\n451:  * {@code\\n452:  * public class KafkaConsumerRunner implements Runnable {\\n453:  *     private final AtomicBoolean closed = new AtomicBoolean(false);\\n454:  *     private final KafkaConsumer consumer;\\n455:  *\\n456:  *     public KafkaConsumerRunner(KafkaConsumer consumer) {\\n457:  *       this.consumer = consumer;\\n458:  *     }\\n459:  *\\n460:  *     @Override\\n461:  *     public void run() {\\n462:  *         try {\\n463:  *             consumer.subscribe(Arrays.asList(\\\"topic\\\"));\\n464:  *             while (!closed.get()) {\\n465:  *                 ConsumerRecords records = consumer.poll(Duration.ofMillis(10000));\\n466:  *                 // Handle new records\\n467:  *             }\\n468:  *         } catch (WakeupException e) {\\n469:  *             // Ignore exception if closing\\n470:  *             if (!closed.get()) throw e;\\n471:  *         } finally {\\n472:  *             consumer.close();\\n473:  *         }\\n474:  *     }\\n475:  *\\n476:  *     // Shutdown hook which can be called from a separate thread\\n477:  *     public void shutdown() {\\n478:  *         closed.set(true);\\n479:  *         consumer.wakeup();\\n480:  *     }\\n481:  * }\\n482:  * }</pre>\\n483:  *\\n484:  * Then in a separate thread, the consumer can be shutdown by setting the closed flag and waking up the consumer.\\n485:  *\\n486:  * <p>\\n487:  * <pre>\\n488:  * {@code\\n489:  *     closed.set(true);\\n490:  *     consumer.wakeup();\\n491:  * }</pre>\\n492:  *\\n493:  * <p>\\n494:  * Note that while it is possible to use thread interrupts instead of {@link #wakeup()} to abort a blocking operation\\n495:  * (in which case, {@link InterruptException} will be raised), we discourage their use since they may cause a clean\\n496:  * shutdown of the consumer to be aborted. Interrupts are mainly supported for those cases where using {@link #wakeup()}\\n497:  * is impossible, e.g. when a consumer thread is managed by code that is unaware of the Kafka client.\\n498:  *\\n499:  * <p>\\n500:  * We have intentionally avoided implementing a particular threading model for processing. This leaves several\\n501:  * options for implementing multi-threaded processing of records.\\n502:  *\\n503:  * <h4>1. One Consumer Per Thread</h4>\\n504:  *\\n505:  * A simple option is to give each thread its own consumer instance. Here are the pros and cons of this approach:\\n506:  * <ul>\\n507:  * <li><b>PRO</b>: It is the easiest to implement\\n508:  * <li><b>PRO</b>: It is often the fastest as no inter-thread co-ordination is needed\\n509:  * <li><b>PRO</b>: It makes in-order processing on a per-partition basis very easy to implement (each thread just\\n510:  * processes messages in the order it receives them).\\n511:  * <li><b>CON</b>: More consumers means more TCP connections to the cluster (one per thread). In general Kafka handles\\n512:  * connections very efficiently so this is generally a small cost.\\n513:  * <li><b>CON</b>: Multiple consumers means more requests being sent to the server and slightly less batching of data\\n514:  * which can cause some drop in I/O throughput.\\n515:  * <li><b>CON</b>: The number of total threads across all processes will be limited by the total number of partitions.\\n516:  * </ul>\\n517:  *\\n518:  * <h4>2. Decouple Consumption and Processing</h4>\\n519:  *\\n520:  * Another alternative is to have one or more consumer threads that do all data consumption and hands off\\n521:  * {@link ConsumerRecords} instances to a blocking queue consumed by a pool of processor threads that actually handle\\n522:  * the record processing.\\n523:  *\\n524:  * This option likewise has pros and cons:\\n525:  * <ul>\\n526:  * <li><b>PRO</b>: This option allows independently scaling the number of consumers and processors. This makes it\\n527:  * possible to have a single consumer that feeds many processor threads, avoiding any limitation on partitions.\\n528:  * <li><b>CON</b>: Guaranteeing order across the processors requires particular care as the threads will execute\\n529:  * independently an earlier chunk of data may actually be processed after a later chunk of data just due to the luck of\\n530:  * thread execution timing. For example each processor thread can have its own queue, and\\n536:  * the consumer threads can hash into these queues using the TopicPartition to ensure in-order consumption and simplify\\n537:  * commit.\\n538:  */\\n539: public class KafkaConsumer<K, V> implements Consumer<K, V> {\\n540: \\n541:     private static final ConsumerDelegateCreator CREATOR = new ConsumerDelegateCreator();\\n542: \\n543:     private final ConsumerDelegate<K, V> delegate;\\n544: \\n545:     /**\\n546:      * A consumer is instantiated by providing a set of key-value pairs as configuration. Another example is bootstrapping upon consumer starting up where there are\\n403:  * a lot of history data to catch up, the applications usually want to get the latest data on some of the topics before consider\\n404:  * fetching other topics.\\n405:  *\\n406:  * <p>\\n407:  * Kafka supports dynamic controlling of consumption flows by using {@link #pause(Collection)} and {@link #resume(Collection)}\\n408:  * to pause the consumption on the specified assigned partitions and resume the consumption\\n409:  * on the specified paused partitions respectively in the future {@link #poll(Duration)} calls.\\n410:  *\\n411:  * <h3>Reading Transactional Messages</h3>\\n412:  *\\n413:  * <p>\\n414:  * Transactions were introduced in Kafka 0.11.0 wherein applications can write to multiple topics and partitions atomically.\\n415:  * In order for this to work, consumers reading from these partitions should be configured to only read committed data.\\n416:  * This can be achieved by setting the {@code isolation.level=read_committed} in the consumer's configuration.\\n417:  *\\n418:  * <p>\\n419:  * In <code>read_committed</code> mode, the consumer will read only those transactional messages which have been\\n420:  * successfully committed. It will continue to read non-transactional messages as before. There is no client-side\\n421:  * buffering in <code>read_committed</code> mode. Instead, the end offset of a partition for a <code>read_committed</code>\\n422:  * consumer would be the offset of the first message in the partition belonging to an open transaction. This offset\\n423:  * is known as the 'Last Stable Offset'(LSO).</p>\\n424:  *\\n425:  * <p>\\n426:  * A {@code read_committed} consumer will only read up to the LSO and filter out any transactional\\n427:  * messages which have been aborted. The LSO also affects the behavior of {@link #seekToEnd(Collection)} and\\n428:  * {@link #endOffsets(Collection)} for {@code read_committed} consumers, details of which are in each method's documentation.\\n429:  * Finally, the fetch lag metrics are also adjusted to be relative to the LSO for {@code read_committed} consumers.\\n430:  *\\n431:  * <p>\\n432:  * Partitions with transactional messages will include commit or abort markers which indicate the result of a transaction.\\n433:  * There markers are not returned to applications, yet have an offset in the log. As a result, applications reading from\\n434:  * topics with transactional messages will see gaps in the consumed offsets. These missing messages would be the transaction\\n435:  * markers, and they are filtered out for consumers in both isolation levels. Additionally, applications using\\n436:  * {@code read_committed} consumers may also see gaps due to aborted transactions, since those messages would not\\n437:  * be returned by the consumer and yet would have valid offsets.\\n438:  *\\n439:  * <h3><a name=\\\"multithreaded\\\">Multi-threaded Processing</a></h3>\\n440:  *\\n441:  * The Kafka consumer is NOT thread-safe. It is the responsibility of the user to ensure that multi-threaded access\\n442:  * is properly synchronized. Un-synchronized access will result in {@link ConcurrentModificationException}.\\n443:  *\\n444:  * <p>\\n445:  * The only exception to this rule is {@link #wakeup()}, which can safely be used from an external thread to\\n446:  * interrupt an active operation. In this case, a {@link org.apache.kafka.common.errors.WakeupException} will be\\n447:  * thrown from the thread blocking on the operation. This can be used to shutdown the consumer from another thread.\\n448:  * The following snippet shows the typical pattern:\\n449:  *\\n450:  * <pre>\\n451:  * {@code\\n452:  * public class KafkaConsumerRunner implements Runnable {\\n453:  *     private final AtomicBoolean closed = new AtomicBoolean(false);\\n454:  *     private final KafkaConsumer consumer;\\n455:  *\\n456:  *     public KafkaConsumerRunner(KafkaConsumer consumer) {\\n457:  *       this.consumer = consumer;\\n458:  *     }\\n459:  *\\n460:  *     @Override\\n461:  *     public void run() {\\n462:  *         try {\\n463:  *             consumer.subscribe(Arrays.asList(\\\"topic\\\"));\\n464:  *             while (!closed.get()) {\\n465:  *                 ConsumerRecords records = consumer.poll(Duration.ofMillis(10000));\\n466:  *                 // Handle new records\\n467:  *             }\\n468:  *         } catch (WakeupException e) {\\n469:  *             // Ignore exception if closing\\n470:  *             if (!closed.get()) throw e;\\n471:  *         } finally {\\n472:  *             consumer.close();\\n473:  *         }\\n474:  *     }\\n475:  *\\n476:  *     // Shutdown hook which can be called from a separate thread\\n477:  *     public void shutdown() {\\n478:  *         closed.set(true);\\n479:  *         consumer.wakeup();\\n480:  *     }\\n481:  * }\\n482:  * }</pre>\\n483:  *\\n484:  * Then in a separate thread, the consumer can be shutdown by setting the closed flag and waking up the consumer.\\n485:  *\\n486:  * <p>\\n487:  * <pre>\\n488:  * {@code\\n489:  *     closed.set(true);\\n490:  *     consumer.wakeup();\\n491:  * }</pre>\\n492:  *\\n493:  * <p>\\n494:  * Note that while it is possible to use thread interrupts instead of {@link #wakeup()} to abort a blocking operation\\n495:  * (in which case, {@link InterruptException} will be raised), we discourage their use since they may cause a clean\\n496:  * shutdown of the consumer to be aborted. The committed offset should be the next message your application will consume,\n1059:      * i.e. {@code nextRecordToBeProcessed.offset()} (or {@link ConsumerRecords#nextOffsets()}).\n1060:      * You should also add the leader epoch as commit metadata, which can be obtained from\n1061:      * {@link ConsumerRecord#leaderEpoch()} or {@link ConsumerRecords#nextOffsets()}.\n1062:      * If automatic group management with {@link #subscribe(Collection)} is used,\n1063:      * then the committed offsets must belong to the currently auto-assigned partitions.\n1064:      * <p>\n1065:      * This is a synchronous commit and will block until either the commit succeeds, an unrecoverable error is\n1066:      * encountered (in which case it is thrown to the caller), or the timeout expires.\n1067:      * <p>\n1068:      * Note that asynchronous offset commits sent previously with the {@link #commitAsync(OffsetCommitCallback)}\n1069:      * (or similar) are guaranteed to have their callbacks invoked prior to completion of this method,\n1070:      * but only when the consumer is using the consumer group protocol.\n1071:      *\n1072:      * @param offsets A map of offsets by partition with associated metadata.
